Invalid Kitabah: Wine and Pork as Consideration
Commentary explaining why kitabah upon wine or pork is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kitabah conditioned on wine or pork and explain the legal basis using the concept of mal mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Invalid consideration renders kitabah fasid — wine and pork are not mutaqawwim for a Muslim.

This lesson examines the invalidity of a kitabah contract when the agreed payment consists of wine or pork. Even if the slave agrees, the contract is spoiled because the consideration lacks legal value (mutaqawwim) for a Muslim master. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract and Invalidating Condition
The passage deals with kitabah where the consideration is wine or pork. First, recognize that kitabah is a contract for gradual manumission, and its validity depends on valid consideration.
2
Understand Why Wine/Pork Are Not Mal Mutaqawwim
In Hanafi fiqh, mutaqawwim property is that which is permissible and has value. For a Muslim, wine and pork are impure and cannot be owned or traded, so they are not considered property in the contract.
Non-Muslims may consider wine as property, but the master here is Muslim.
3
Apply the Principle to Determine Contract Status
Since the consideration is not valid property, the contract is spoiled. The Hanafi ruling classifies this kitabah as fasid (defective), not batil, because the defect is in the consideration, which can potentially be replaced.
4
Recognize the Scope of the Ruling
The ruling applies when the master is Muslim and the slave is either Muslim or non-Muslim, provided they are in Dar al-Islam. The contract is invalid due to the master's inability to receive such consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كاتبه على خمر أو خنزير شروع في الكتابة الفاسدة بعد الصحيحة رحمه لأن الفاسدة تتلو الصحيحة يعني لو كاتب المسلم عبده المسلم أو الكافر في دار الإسلام على خمر أو خنزير فالكتابة فاسدة لأن الخمر والخنزير ليس بمال في حق المسلم فلا يصلح عوضاً فيفسد العقد لأن تسمية ما ليس بمتقوم في حق ما يحتاج فيه إلى تسمية البدل توجب فساد
And if he contracts kitabah upon wine or pork, this is a start of the invalid kitabah after the valid, may Allah have mercy on him, because the invalid follows the valid. Meaning, if a Muslim contracts kitabah with his Muslim slave or a non-Muslim slave in the Abode of Islam upon wine or pork, the kitabah is invalid because wine and pork are not property (mal) in the right of a Muslim, so they are not suitable as consideration, so the contract is spoiled because naming something that is not valuable in a context that requires naming consideration leads to spoilage.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion on invalid kitabah. The commentator introduces the topic by comparing it to valid kitabah. The invalidity arises when the consideration is something that is not considered wealth (mal mutaqawwim) in Islamic law for the contracting party. For a Muslim, wine and pork are not property because they are impure and cannot be owned or traded. Therefore, if a Muslim master contracts with his Muslim slave (or even a non-Muslim slave in Dar al-Islam) for wine or pork as the payment, the contract is void (fasid). The reason is that the consideration must be valuable property; otherwise, the contract is invalid. This is a clear ruling based on the legal principle that the subject matter must be valid property.

Key Terms
فاسد (fasid): A contract that is defective but may become valid by rectification of the defect; here used for kitabah with invalid consideration.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that is legally permissible and has value; wine and pork are not mutaqawwim for Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kitabah upon wine invalid for a Muslim master?
A) Because wine is not a tangible object.
B) Because wine is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for a Muslim.
C) Because wine cannot be delivered.
D) Because wine has no market value.

Key Takeaway
Kitabah is fasid if the consideration is not mutaqawwim for the master.

Kitābah with Invalid Consideration: Khamr and Khinzīr
Ruling on kitābah with invalid consideration and its consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a kitābah contract specifies an invalid consideration (khamr/khinzīr), identify the conditions under which the slave can become free, and distinguish between fāsid and bāṭil contracts in this context.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a kitābah on khamr or khinzīr is invalid (fāsid), and freedom requires paying the value, not the prohibited item.

This passage tackles a critical fiqh issue: what happens when a master and slave agree on a kitābah contract where the payment is something Islam does not recognize as property—like wine or pork? The Hanafi school rules that such a contract is fāsid (invalid) because the consideration lacks the quality of māl. However, the ruling comes with important exceptions and detailed consequences, which we will unpack step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
The main rule: if a slave pays khamr, he does not become free; only paying the qīmah (value) effects freedom. Understand that the contract is fāsid, not bāṭil.
2
Distinguish Between Types of Consideration
Compare khamr/khinzīr (fāsid) with maytah/dam (bāṭil). In the latter, payment does not free even with value unless an oath is made.
3
Examine the Role of the Slave's Faith and Location
The ruling changes if the slave is a disbeliever (contract valid) or if the contract is made in Dār al-Ḥarb with ignorance (valid due to ignorance).
4
Understand Procedural Differences Between Jā'iz and Fāsid
In a fāsid kitābah, the master can cancel without consent; in a jā'iz, cancellation requires the slave's consent. The slave can cancel either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب العقد كالبيع، ولو أدى الخمر لا يعتق، ولو أدى القيمة عتق اهـ. والظاهر أن المسلم باشر فلو وكل ذمياً في كتابة عبده المسلم على خمر أو خنزير فالكتابة فاسدة لأنه لو كان كافراً وأسلم تفسد فإذا فسدت بالإسلام في البقاء ففي الابتداء أولى ولو كاتب عبده الكافر على خمر أو خنزير فالظاهر أنها صحيحة ويعتق بأداء ذلك ولا سعاية عليه أخذاً . من قولهم يملك أن يوكل فيما لا يملكه. وقيدنا بقولنا على خمر أو خنزير لأنه لو كاتبه على ميتة أو دم فالكتابة باطلة، فإن أدى لا يعتق إلا إذا قال إن أديت إلي فأنت حر فيعتق لأجل اليمين لا لأجل الكتابة؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي أيضاً. وفي المحيط : لو كاتب على خمر أو خنزير عتق بأداء القيمة قبل إبطال القاضي لأن الكتابة إذا فسدت لفساد التسمية لكونه ليس بمال انعقدت الكتابة على القيمة فيتعلق العتق بأدائها .اهـ. وفي المنتقى : لو كاتب على ألف ورطل من الخمر فهي فاسدة. وفي المبسوط : لو كاتبها على ألف على أن كل ولد تلده لك يد فهي فاسدة، وإن ولدت في الفاسدة ثم أدت عتق ولدها معها. وفي شرح الطحاوي: والفرق بين الجائزة والفاسدة أن في الفاسدة للمولى أن يرده إلى الرق ويفسخ الكتابة بغير رضاه، وفي الجائزة لا يفسخ إلا برضا العبد وللعبد أن يفسخ في الجائزة والفاسدة، جميعاً بغير رضا المولى. وفي المبسوط : ولو كاتبه كتابة فاسدة ثم مات المولى فأدى المكاتب إلى ورثته عتق استحساناً اهـ . قيدنا بدار الإسلام لأن المسلم الذي كان في دار الإسلام لو دخل دار الحرب فكاتب عبده المسلم والكافر على خمر أو خنزير فالحكم كما لو كان في دار الإسلام وكاتب من يعلم بالأحكام ولو تقديراً، فلو أسلم في دار الحرب ولم تبلغه الأحكام فكاتب على خمر أو خنزير فالظاهر أنها صحيحة ويعتق بأداء ذلك ولا سعاية لأنه يعذر بالجهل في هذه الحالة.
The book of Kitābah. The contract is like a sale. If he pays khamr, he does not become free. If he pays the value, he becomes free, end. It is apparent that if a Muslim directly conducts the kitābah, if he appoints a dhimmī as agent to write his Muslim slave for khamr or pig, the kitābah is fāsid because if he were a disbeliever and becomes Muslim, it becomes invalid. If it becomes invalid by Islam remaining, then in the beginning it is more so. If he writes his disbelieving slave for khamr or pig, apparently it is valid and he becomes free by paying that, and no sa‘āyah is required, taken from their statement: he has the authority to appoint an agent in what he does not own. We restricted our statement 'for khamr or pig' because if he wrote him for carrion or blood, the kitābah is void. If he pays, he does not become free unless he says: 'If you pay to me, you are free.' Then he becomes free due to the oath, not due to the kitābah. Likewise in Sharḥ al-Ṭaḥāwī. In al-Muḥīṭ: If he writes [the kitābah] for khamr or pig, he becomes free by paying the value before the judge invalidates it, because when the kitābah becomes invalid due to invalidity of the named consideration because it is not property, the kitābah is concluded upon the value, so freedom is attached to its payment. End. In al-Muntaqā: If he writes him for one thousand and a pound of khamr, it is invalid. In al-Mabsūṭ: If he writes her for one thousand on the condition that every child she gives birth to is for you as a hand, it is invalid. If she gives birth during the invalid [kitābah] then pays, her child becomes free with her. In Sharḥ al-Ṭaḥāwī: The difference between the valid and invalid [kitābah] is that in the invalid, the master has the right to return him to slavery and cancel the kitābah without his consent. In the valid, he cannot cancel except with the slave's consent, and the slave has the right to cancel in both valid and invalid without the master's consent. In al-Mabsūṭ: If he wrote him a fāsid kitābah, then the master died, and the mukātab paid to the heirs, he becomes free by istiḥsān, end. We restricted [the ruling] to Dār al-Islām because if a Muslim who was in Dār al-Islām enters Dār al-Ḥarb and writes his Muslim and disbelieving slave for khamr or pig, the ruling is as if he were in Dār al-Islām and wrote someone who knows the rulings, even by estimation. If he becomes Muslim in Dār al-Ḥarb and the rulings have not reached him, and he writes for khamr or pig, apparently it is valid, and he becomes free by paying that, and no sa‘āyah is required because he is excused due to ignorance in this situation.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on kitābah (contract of manumission) where the consideration is something invalid like khamr (wine) or khinzīr (pig). The opening statement 'العقد كالبيع' establishes that a kitābah contract is analogous to a sale. The core ruling is that if a mukātab pays khamr, he does not become free; only if he pays the value (qīmah) does he become free. This is because the consideration must be māl (property), and khamr is not considered property in Islamic law. However, the text elaborates several scenarios. If a Muslim directly contracts kitābah with his Muslim slave for khamr or khinzīr, it is invalid (fāsid). If a Muslim appoints a dhimmī as agent to do so, it is also invalid because even if the agent were a disbeliever who later becomes Muslim, the contract would become invalid in its continuity, so in its inception it is more so. Conversely, if a Muslim master writes his disbelieving slave for khamr or khinzīr, the kitābah is valid and the slave becomes free by paying those items, and no sa‘āyah (pursuit for earnings) is required. This is based on the principle that the master could appoint an agent for what he does not own, and here the slave's own earnings are not directly owned by the master. The text then distinguishes between kitābah on khamr/khinzīr versus maytah (carrion) or dam (blood); in the latter, the kitābah is batil (void), and payment does not effect freedom unless the master additionally makes an oath. The difference between jā'iz (valid) and fāsid (invalid) kitābah is explained: in the invalid, the master can cancel the contract and return the slave to slavery without his consent; in the valid, the master cannot cancel without the slave's consent, but the slave can cancel either type without the master's consent. Finally, the text addresses the scenario of Dār al-Ḥarb: if a Muslim in Dār al-Islām enters Dār al-Ḥarb and writes his slave for khamr/khinzīr, the ruling is as if in Dār al-Islām. But if a person becomes Muslim in Dār al-Ḥarb without knowledge of the rulings, his kitābah for khamr/khinzīr is valid due to his ignorance.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave pays a sum and becomes free.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a kitābah contract.
خمر (khamr): Wine or any intoxicating drink; not considered property in Hanafi law.
فاسدة (fāsidah): An invalid contract due to a defect in the consideration or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim master writes his Muslim slave for a consideration of khamr, what happens if the slave actually pays the khamr?
A) He becomes free because he fulfilled the contract terms.
B) He does not become free; instead, he must pay the value (qīmah) to become free.
C) The contract is batil and he remains a slave forever.
D) He becomes free but the master must refund the khamr.

Key Takeaway
In a kitābah, the consideration must be valid property; paying an invalid item like khamr does not free the slave unless the value is paid or a special exception applies.

Invalid Kitābah Consideration: The Slave's Own Value or Another's Property
Ruling that kitābah on own value or another's property is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why kitābah is invalid when the consideration is the slave's own value or a specific item owned by another, and distinguish between cases where the consideration is specified or unspecified.

Core Idea
Kitābah requires a known, deliverable consideration — uncertainty in value or inability to deliver another's property invalidates the contract.

This lesson examines two scenarios where a kitābah contract becomes invalid: when the consideration is the slave's own market value (unknown in amount and genus) and when it is a specific item owned by a third party (cannot be delivered). The rulings are grounded in the principles of jahāla (uncertainty) and the condition of ability to deliver. Understanding these principles helps ensure that kitābah contracts are validly structured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules that kitābah on the slave's own value (qīmat nafsihi) or on a specific item belonging to another (ʿayn li-ghayrihi) is invalid (fāsid).
This is the core ruling: fāsid, not bāṭil, because the contract can potentially be corrected by specifying a valid consideration.
2
Locate the dalīl (evidence)
The proof is rational: for the slave's own value, the consideration is unknown in amount and genus, analogous to kitābah for a garment or animal. For another's property, the slave cannot guarantee delivery, which is a condition for validity in contracts admitting rescission.
The analogy (qiyas) is explicit: 'صارت كما لو كاتب على ثوب أو دابة'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah (legal reasoning)
Jahāla fāḥisha in both quantity and genus renders the consideration unenforceable in the dhimmah. Inability to deliver the specified item makes the specification itself invalid. The text distinguishes between intentional (qaṣdan) and implicit (ḍimnan) obligations.
The key principle: 'ما هو مجهول الجنس لا يثبت في الذمة'.
4
Note the ikhtilāf (disagreement)
Within the Hanafi school, Abū Ḥanīfa permits the specification if the owner of the item gives permission, while Muḥammad (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) holds that permission does not validate it. Al-Ḥasan permits kitābah on another's property outright. Zufar differs on the female slave's 'judgment' case.
The muftā bihī is the ẓāhir al-riwāyah: invalid even with permission.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ (preferred view)
The relied-upon position is that the contract is fāsid, and if the slave pays the value or the item later, freedom can occur by agreement or by court appraisal, but the initial specification is defective.
The author of al-Muḥīṭ notes that payment of the value can effect freedom if both agree or appraisal settles it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (أو على قيمته أو عين لغيره يعني الكتابة فاسدة إذا كاتبه على قيمة نفسه أو على عين لغيره، أما على قيمة نفسه فإنها مجهولة القدر لأنها تختلف باختلاف المقومين وجنسها كذلك مجهول فصار كما لو كاتب على ثوب أو دابة لأن الثوب والدابة أجناس مختلفة، وما هو مجهول الجنس لا يثبت في الذمة حتى في النكاح، ولأن موجب الكتابة الفاسدة القيمة بالتنصيص عليها، ولا يقال لو كاتبه على عبده يجوز ويجب عليه عبد وسط أو قيمة ولو أبي أخذ القيمة يجبر عليها ولو كانت الكتابة في القيمة فاسدة لما صح ذلك لأنا نقول : القيمة في مسألة الكتاب ثبت قصداً وفيما ذكرت ثبت ضمناً ويتسامح في الضمني ما لم يتسامح في القصدي. وفي المحيط وإن أدى القيمة عتق لأنها وإن فسدت يبقى تعليق العتق بالأداء فمتى تصادقا على أن المؤدي قيمته ثبت ذلك بالتصادق وإن اختلفا، فإن اتفق اثنان المقومين على شيء يجعل ذلك قيمة له، وإن اختلفا فقوم أحدهما بألف والآخر بالأكثر لا يعتق لم يؤد أقصى قيمته. ولو كاتب أمة على حكمة أو حكمها لم يجز ولا يعتق بأداء قيمتها خلافاً لزفر. قوله «أو على عين لغيره كالثوب والعبد وغيرهما من المكيل والموزون غير النقدين، والمراد به شيء يتعين بالتعيين حتى لو كاتب على دراهم أو دنانير وهي لغيره تجوز الكتابة لأنها لا تتعين بالتعيين. وعن الحسن تجوز الكتابة على مال الغير. وجه ظاهر الرواية أن العين في المعاوضات معقود عليها والقدرة على تسليم المعقود عليه شرط للصحة في العقود التي تحتمل الفسخ وتسليم تلك العين ليست في قدرته فلا تصح تسميته بخلاف ما إذا كان البدل غير معين لأنه معقود عليه فلا يشترط القدرة عليه ولو أجاز صاحب العين ذلك روي عن محمد أنه لا يجوز وهو ظاهر الرواية ؛ كذا في العتابية. وعن الإمام أنه يجوز أجاز أو لم يجز غير أنه عند الإجارة يجب تسليم العين وعند عدم الإجارة يجب تسليم القيمة. وروي الثاني عن الإمام أنه لو ملك القيمة فأدى لم يعتق إلا أن يكون قال له المولى إن حر. وذكر صاحب الإملاء أنه يعتق بالدفع قال المولى إن أديت إلي أديت فأنت فأنت أو لم حر يقل كما لو كاتب على خمر. وجه ما ذكر عن الإمام أن العين لم يصر بدلا في هذا العقد لأنه لا يقدر على تسليمه فلا ينعقد بالعقد أصلاً. بتسميته
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (or on his value, or on a specific item belonging to another, meaning the kitābah is invalid if he writes him for his own value or for a specific item belonging to another. As for his own value, it is unknown in amount because it differs according to appraisers, and its genus is likewise unknown, so it becomes as if he wrote him for a garment or an animal, because garments and animals are different genera. And what is unknown in genus is not established in the dhimmah, even in marriage. And because the requirement of an invalid kitābah is the value specified therein. And it cannot be said: if he writes him for his slave, it is permissible and he must give an average slave or its value, and if he refuses to take the value he is compelled to take it, and if the kitābah on value is invalid, why would that be valid? For we say: the value in the matter of kitābah is established intentionally, and in what you mentioned it is established implicitly, and what is tolerated implicitly is not tolerated intentionally. In al-Muḥīṭ: If he pays the value, he becomes free because even if it is invalid, the suspension of freedom upon payment remains. When they both agree that what is paid is its value, it is established by agreement; if they disagree, then if two appraisers agree on something, that is taken as the value; if they disagree—one appraises it at one thousand and another at more—he does not become free because he did not pay the maximum value. If he writes a female slave on her judgment (ḥukm) or her own judgment, it is not permissible, and she does not become free by paying her value, contrary to Zufar. His statement: (or on a specific item belonging to another, like a garment, a slave, and other measurable and weighable items excluding the two metals (gold and silver). What is intended is something that is specified by identification. So if he writes him for dirhams or dinars that belong to another, the kitābah is permissible because they are not specified by identification. Al-Ḥasan reported that kitābah on another's property is permissible. The justification of the apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) is that the specific item in exchanges is the object of contract, and ability to deliver the object of contract is a condition for validity in contracts that admit rescission. And the ability to deliver that specific item is not within his power, so its specification is not valid, unlike the case where the consideration is unspecified because it is [only] the object of the obligation, and ability [to deliver] is not required. If the owner of the item gives permission, it is reported from Muḥammad that it is not permissible, and that is the apparent narration. Likewise in al-ʿAtābiyyah. And from the Imām (Abū Ḥanīfa) that it is permissible whether he permits or not, except that if he gives permission, the specific item must be delivered, and if he does not permit, the value must be delivered. And the second is reported from the Imām: if he comes to own the value and pays it, he does not become free unless the master says to him 'you are free'. The author of al-Imlā' mentioned that he becomes free upon payment, whether the master said 'if you pay to me you are free' or not, just as in the case of kitābah on khamr. The justification of what is reported from the Imām is that the specific item did not become a consideration in this contract because he cannot deliver it, so it is not concluded by the contract at all through its specification.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on kitābah when the consideration is either the slave's own value (qīmat nafsihi) or a specific item owned by another person (ʿayn li-ghayrihi). The text explains that such a kitābah is invalid (fāsid). For the slave's own value, the problem is jahāla (ignorance) in both quantity and genus: value varies by appraiser, and the genus of value is unknown (since it could be any property). This is analogous to kitābah for a garment or an animal, which are different genera. Anything with unknown genus is not established in the dhimmah (obligation). The text addresses an objection: if kitābah on a slave (as consideration) is valid, and then an average slave or its value is owed, why is kitābah on value invalid? The answer is that in the valid case, the value is implied (ḍimnan) and tolerances are accepted for implied matters, but here it is the primary intended (qaṣdan) consideration. If the slave pays the value, he becomes free by agreement; if they disagree, two appraisers decide, and the slave must pay the maximum appraised value. For kitābah on a specific item belonging to another, the ruling is similar: the consideration is invalid because the slave cannot guarantee delivery of that specific item. However, if the consideration is dirhams or dinars (which are fungible and not specified by identification), the kitābah is valid. An alternative view from al-Ḥasan permits kitābah on another's property. The learned authorities (ʿUlamā) discuss whether permission from the owner of the item changes the ruling. According to the apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāyah) from Muḥammad, permission does not validate the specification. But from Abū Ḥanīfa, if permission is given, the item must be delivered; if not, the value must be paid. The text ends with a distinction: according to a report from Abū Ḥanīfa, the kitābah does not even conclude with the specification of that item because it cannot be delivered, so it is as if no contract was made.

Key Terms
قيمته (qīmatuhu): The slave's own market value; unknown and thus invalid as consideration.
عين لغيره (ʿayn li-ghayrihi): A specific item owned by someone other than the contracting parties.
جهالة (jahālah): Uncertainty (ignorance) in the object of contract, leading to invalidity.
كتابة فاسدة (kitāba fāsida): An invalid kitābah contract that may still produce some effects (e.g., freedom upon payment by agreement) but is defective in its consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kitābah on a specific item owned by another (e.g., a garment belonging to Zayd) invalid according to the ẓāhir al-riwāyah?
A) Because the item is not owned by the slave.
B) Because the slave cannot guarantee delivery of that specific item.
C) Because the item might be worth more than the slave.
D) Because the item is a fungible good.

Key Takeaway
Kitābah on an uncertain or undeliverable consideration is invalid; always specify a known, deliverable consideration.

Kitābah with Condition to Return a Young Slave (intra-Hanafi dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kitābah with condition to return a young slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad on kitābah conditioned on returning a young slave, explain the reasoning based on jahāla and ṣafqah fī ṣafqah, and compare it with Abū Yūsuf's position.

Core Idea
Kitābah plus a condition to return a young slave is invalid due to uncertainty and bundling kitābah with sale.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a kitābah contract where the master stipulates that the slave pay one hundred dirhams and also return a young slave. Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad rule the contract invalid, while Abū Yūsuf considers it valid with a proportional reduction. The central issue is whether the condition creates uncertainty (jahāla) and constitutes a prohibited package deal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi majority position
Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad: the kitābah is invalid (fāsid). They argue the total consideration is unknown because the value of the young slave is not fixed, and the contract combines kitābah and sale (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah) which is prohibited.
2
Identify the opposing view (Abū Yūsuf)
Abū Yūsuf: the kitābah is valid. He argues that any valid contract can have exceptions; the hundred dirhams are divided between the value of the mukātab and the young slave, and the portion for the young slave is dropped.
3
Analyse Abū Yūsuf's proof
Abū Yūsuf relies on the principle that whatever can be included can be excepted. He sees the condition as a simple exception, not a separate sale.
His position is analogous to cases where uncertainty is minor and tolerated.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority responds that the exception of a young slave from dirhams is invalid because the young slave's value must be assessed, and that introduces fatal uncertainty. Moreover, the contract is a bundle of two different transactions.
They also argue that the exception leads to jahāla fāhisha (excessive uncertainty).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the majority view, the contract is void and must be restructured as two separate contracts. Under Abū Yūsuf, the contract is enforced with a reduction in the amount. The muftā bihi is the majority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (أو بمائة ليرد عليه سيده وصيفاً (فسد) قوله «فسد» هذا أخبر لقوله قال «وإن كاتب» يعني لو كاتبه على مائة ليرد عليه سيده وصيفاً فالكتابة فاسدة على قول الإمام ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف : الكتابة صحيحة وتقسم المائة على قيمة المكاتب والوصيف الوسط فما أصاب الوصيفة الوسط يسقط عنه ويكون مكاتب وتقسم المائة بما بقي لأن كل ما جاز إيراد العقد عليه جاز استثناؤه من العقد والكتابة ولهما أن بدل الكتابة مجهول القدر فلا كما إذا كاتبه على قيمة الوصيف هذا لأن الأصل المذكور صحيح فيما إذا الاستثناء صح يؤدي إلى فساد العقد، وهنا استثناء العبد من الدراهم غير صحيح لأن الوصيف يصح من غير أن لا يمكن استثناؤه من الدنانير إلا باعتبار القيمة وتسمية القيمة تفسد العقد، ولأن هذا عقد يشتمل على الكتابة والبيع لأن ما كان من الدنانير بأداء الوصيف الذي يرده المولى بيع وما كان بمقابلة رقبة المكاتب هو مكاتبة لا فيبطل الجهالة المثمن والثمن فهو صفقة في صفقة فلا يجوز للنهي عنها، وإذا كاتبه على حيوان وبين جنسه كالعبد والفرس ولم يبين أنه تركي أو هندي ولا الوصف أنه رديء جازت الكتابة ويصرف إلى الوسط وقدر الإمام الوسط بما جيد أو قيمته أربعون. وقالا : هو على قدر غلاء السعر ورخصه ولا ينظر في قيمة الوسط إلى قيمة المكاتب ويجبر على قبول قيمته وإنما يصح العقد مع الجهالة لأنها يسيرة فصار كما لو كاتب وجعل الأجل الحصاد، ولقائل أن يقول : مقتضى هذا التعليل أن لا تصح الكتابة فيما إذا كاتبه على مائة على أن يرد عليه عبداً معيناً لأن قيمة المعين مجهولة جهالة فاحشة ولهذا لو كاتبه عليها لم يصح وقد صرحوا فيما إذا شرط على أن يرد عليه عبداً معيناً أن بالاتفاق، نقله يصح في الكافي والدرر والغرر، وفي المبسوط ولو كاتبه على خمر أو خنزير فسد فإن أداه قبل أن :
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (or with one hundred on condition that he return a young slave to his master, (invalid)). His statement 'invalid' informs his statement 'and if he writes'. Meaning: if he writes him [the kitābah] for one hundred dirhams on condition that he return a young slave to his master, the kitābah is invalid according to the statement of the Imām (Abū Ḥanīfa) and Muḥammad. What corresponds to the average young slave is dropped, and he becomes a mukātab for the remainder of the hundred. Because whatever is permissible to include in a contract is permissible to except from the contract and kitābah. This is because the mentioned principle is valid when the exception does not lead to invalidity of the contract. Here, the exception of the slave from the dirhams is not valid because the young slave is valid [as consideration] from the fact that it cannot be excepted from dinars except by virtue of value, and specifying the value invalidates the contract. And because this contract includes both kitābah and sale: the portion of the dirhams that corresponds to the young slave to be returned is a sale, and the portion corresponding to the slave's own person is kitābah. So it is a package deal (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah), which is prohibited. If he writes him for an animal and specifies its genus like a slave or a horse, but does not specify whether it is Turkish or Indian, nor its description as deficient, the kitābah is valid, and it is assigned to the average. The Imām determined the average as what is good or its value is forty. The two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) said: it depends on the high and low price, and the value of the mukātab is not considered in the value of the average, and he is compelled to accept its value. The contract is valid despite the uncertainty because it is minor, as if he wrote him and set the term as harvest time. One might say: the implication of this reasoning is that kitābah is not valid in the case where he writes him for one hundred on condition that he return a specific slave, because the value of the specific slave is excessively unknown. For this reason, if he wrote him [the kitābah] for it [the specific slave], it would not be valid. They explicitly stated that when he stipulates that he return a specific slave, it is, by agreement, valid? It is reported in al-Kāfī, al-Durar, and al-Ghurar. Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a kitābah contract where the master stipulates that the slave must pay one hundred dirhams and also return a young slave (wasīf) to the master. According to Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad, such a contract is invalid (fāsid) because the total consideration is unknown: it is a combination of a fixed sum (one hundred) and the value of the young slave, which is unknown. Additionally, the contract involves both a sale (the return of the young slave) and kitābah, making it a prohibited package deal (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah). Abū Yūsuf argues that the kitābah is valid: the hundred dirhams should be divided proportionally between the value of the mukātab and the value of the young slave; the portion corresponding to the young slave is dropped, and the slave becomes a mukātab for the remainder. Abū Yūsuf's reasoning is that whatever can be included in a contract can be excepted. The text then discusses a related case: if the kitābah is for an animal with its genus specified (e.g., a slave or horse) but without further description, the kitābah is valid due to minor uncertainty, and the average is taken. The Imām (Abū Ḥanīfa) judges the average by value (e.g., 40), while the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) consider market price fluctuations. The passage ends with a note that if the kitābah is for khamr or khinzīr, it is invalid, and the slave's payment before the judge's invalidation is discussed (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
وصيف (waṣīf): A young slave (boy or girl) to be returned to the master as part of the consideration.
صفقة في صفقة (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah): A bundled contract combining two different transactions, prohibited in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the slave cannot own property to return a young slave.
D) Because the master must not benefit from the slave's work.

Key Takeaway
Kitābah with a condition to return a young slave is invalid (fāsid) because the total consideration is unknown and the contract bundles kitābah with sale (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah).

Kitabah on Another's Property: Specified vs Unspecified Cash
Ruling on kitabah with third-party cash, specified vs unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between specified and unspecified cash in kitabah and state the Hanafi ruling on validity when the cash belongs to someone else.

Core Idea
If kitabah payment is unspecified, it's valid; if specified (without permission), it's invalid due to lack of ability to deliver.

Kitabah is a contract where a slave agrees to pay a sum in exchange for freedom. But what happens if the agreed cash belongs to a third party? The answer hinges on whether the cash is specified (like particular coins) or just generic. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the role of owner's permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Start by identifying the act: a slave (mukatab) and a master agree on a kitabah where the payment is to be made in cash (dirhams or dinars) that belongs to a third party, not the master.
The cash can be either specified (e.g., those specific coins) or unspecified (e.g., any dirhams of that type).
2
Classify the cash as specified or unspecified
In Hanafi fiqh, coins of standard weight and purity are considered specified once physically identified (e.g., 'this bag of dirhams'). If the contract refers to generic dirhams, they are unspecified. This classification determines the ruling.
Unspecified cash can be acquired later; specified cash requires immediate ownership.
3
Apply the condition of ability to deliver
The general rule: in contracts that admit rescission (like kitabah), the ability to deliver the subject matter at the time of contract is a condition for validity. The slave cannot deliver cash he does not own, so if the cash is specified and belongs to another, the contract is invalid without that owner's permission.
This is the proof (dalil aqli) given in the text: ability to deliver is a condition.
4
Consider the effect of owner's permission
If the owner of the specified cash permits its use, there is a difference of opinion. Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah) holds the contract invalid even with permission, because the slave lacked ability at the time of contract. Imam Abu Hanifa holds it valid, with obligation to deliver the cash or its value. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Imam Muhammad's: invalid.
Permission does not retroactively cure the initial incapacity to deliver.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب النقدين، والمراد به شيء يتعين بالتعيين حتى لو كاتب على دراهم أو دنانير وهي لغيره تجوز الكتابة لأنها لا تتعين بالتعيين. وعن الحسن تجوز الكتابة على مال الغير. وجه ظاهر الرواية أن العين في المعاوضات معقود عليه والقدرة على تسليم المعقود عليه شرط للصحة في العقود التي تحتمل الفسخ وتسليم تلك العين ليست في قدرته فلا تصح تسميته بخلاف ما إذا كان البدل غير معين لأنه معقود عليه فلا يشترط القدرة عليه ولو أجاز صاحب العين ذلك روي عن محمد أنه لا يجوز وهو ظاهر الرواية ؛ كذا في العتابية. وعن الإمام أنه يجوز أجاز أو لم يجز غير أنه عند الإجارة يجب تسليم العين وعند عدم الإجارة يجب تسليم القيمة. وروي الثاني عن الإمام أنه لو ملك القيمة فأدى لم يعتق إلا أن يكون قال له المولى إن حر. وذكر صاحب الإملاء أنه يعتق بالدفع قال المولى إن أديت إلي أديت فأنت فأنت أو لم حر يقل كما لو كاتب على خمر. وجه ما ذكر عن الإمام أن العين لم يصر بدلا في هذا العقد لأنه لا يقدر على تسليمه فلا ينعقد بالعقد أصلاً. بتسميته
Kitab al-Mukatab: Al-Naqdain (the two metals). What is meant is something that becomes specific by specification. So if he enters into kitabah on dirhams or dinars that belong to someone else, the kitabah is valid because they are not specified by specification. From al-Hasan: kitabah on another's property is valid. The explanation of the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that the specific item in commutative contracts is the subject matter, and the ability to deliver the subject matter is a condition for validity in contracts that admit rescission, and he does not have the ability to deliver that specific item, so its specification is invalid, contrary to the case when the substitute is unspecified because there is no condition of ability over it. If the owner of the item permits it, it is narrated from Muhammad that it is not valid, and that is the apparent narration. Similarly in al-Atabiyyah. From the Imam (Abu Hanifa): it is valid whether he permits or not, except that when there is permission (ijara?) the item must be delivered, and when there is no permission, the value must be delivered. The second is narrated from the Imam that if he possessed the value and paid it, he is not freed unless the master said to him: 'You are free.' The author of al-Imla' mentioned that he is freed by payment, whether the master said 'If you pay to me, you are free' or did not say, just as if he entered into kitabah on wine. The explanation of what is narrated from the Imam is that the specific item does not become a substitute in this contract because he cannot deliver it, so it does not conclude at all by its specification.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on Kitab al-Mukatab. It addresses a specific scenario: when a slave enters into a kitabah contract agreeing to pay a specified amount of cash (dirhams or dinars) that actually belongs to a third party, not the master. The key distinction is whether the cash is specified (mu'ayyan) or unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan). In the Hanafi school, if the cash is unspecified (like generic dirhams), it is valid because the slave can acquire and deliver them. If the cash is specified (like specific coins), the contract is invalid because the slave cannot deliver something he doesn't own. The passage then discusses a disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the case where the owner of the specified cash permits its use. According to Zahir al-Riwayah (the relied-upon narration), Imam Muhammad holds the contract invalid even with permission because the slave still lacks the ability to deliver at the time of contract. Imam Abu Hanifa holds it valid, but with the obligation to deliver the cash if permission is given, otherwise its value. The passage clarifies that the Zahir al-Riwayah position is the one taken in the madhhab, and the mufta bihi is that the contract is invalid without the master's permission. This ruling stems from the general principle that ability to deliver the subject matter is a condition for validity in contracts that admit rescission.

Key Terms
النقدين (al-naqdayn): The two monetary metals: gold and silver, i.e., cash dirhams and dinars.
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The most authentic and relied-upon narrations within the Hanafi school, transmitted by Imam Muḥammad al-Shaybānī in his six books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, if a slave enters kitabah on specified dirhams belonging to a third party, and the owner later permits, what is the ruling?
A) The kitabah is valid regardless.
B) The kitabah is invalid even with permission.
C) The kitabah is valid only if the slave pays the value.
D) The kitabah becomes valid if the master agrees.

Key Takeaway
In kitabah, the ability to deliver the subject matter at contract time is a condition; for specified cash, this cannot be fulfilled if it belongs to another, so the contract is invalid even with later permission.

Ruling on Payment in Invalid Kitabah
Explaining that in an invalid kitabah the slave must pay the value, not less than named, and can be increased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for payment in an invalid kitabah contract and apply it to cases where the named amount differs from the slave's value, including special conditions for service, wine, and garment.

Core Idea
In invalid kitabah, the slave pays the greater of the named amount or his value, never less than the named amount.

This lesson explains a crucial rule from the Hanafi school concerning invalid kitabah contracts. When a master contracts kitabah with his slave on an invalid consideration—such as perpetual service or a gift—the contract is fasid (defective), but the manumission can still be valid upon payment. The key is determining how much the slave must pay: the named amount sets a floor, while the slave's value sets a ceiling for additional payment if higher. We will dissect the passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq to understand the reasoning and apply it to various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its validity
Recognize that kitabah on invalid considerations like perpetual service or a gift renders the contract fasid. The manumission is not automatic but contingent on proper payment.
Service (khidmah) is not a valid substitute in exchange contracts.
2
Understand the payment rule
The slave must pay the greater of the named amount (musamma) or his value (qimah). If value is less, he pays the named amount; if value is more, he pays the value (i.e., the named amount plus the excess).
The master's consent sets a floor; the slave's willingness to pay more for freedom allows increase.
3
Apply to specific examples
Work through the examples: (a) kitabah on 1000 + service forever → pay 1000 or value whichever greater. (b) kitabah on 1000 + wine → must pay both because wine is mal. (c) kitabah on a garment → contract is batil (void) due to excessive uncertainty, so no manumission by paying garment alone; only if explicitly conditionalized.
The distinction between fasid and batil: fasid allows manumission on correct payment; batil does not.
4
Distinguish the garment case
In kitabah on an unspecified garment (thawb), the ignorance (juhala) is excessive, making the contract batil. Paying the garment or its value does not effect freedom unless the master explicitly suspends freedom on payment.
The effect of juhala in fasid contracts does not invalidate the contract; in batil it does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولم ينقص عن المسمى وزيد عليه) هذه المسألة لا تعلق لها بمسألة الخمر بل مسألة مبتدأة ومعناها : كاتب عبده على ألف وخدمته أبداً أو على ألف وهدية فالخدمة أبداً والهدية لا تصلح بدلاً فالعقد فاسد، فإذا أدى الألف عتق، فإن كان الألف قدر قيمته لم يبق للمولى عليه سبيل، وإن كان قيمته أكثر رجع عليه السيد بالزيادة، وإن كانت الألف أكثر من قيمته فلا يعتق إلا بدفعها، ولو كاتبه على ألف ورطل من الخمر لا يعتق حتى يدفع الألف والرطل من الخمر؛ كذا في المحيط مختصراً . قال الشارح: لأنه عقد فاسد فيجب عليه قيمته بالغة ما بلغت غير أن المولى لم يرض أن يعتقه بأقل مما سمی فلا ينقص منه إن نقصت قيمته عن المسمى، والعبد يرضى بالزيادة حتى ينال شرف الحرية فيزاد عليه إذا زادت قيمته لينال الشرف وفيما إذا كاتبه على قيمته يعتق بأدائها لأنه هو البدل في الفاسد ذكرها أو لم يذكرها فأمكن اعتبار معنى العتق فيه وأثر الجهالة في الفاسد لا في إبطال العقد بخلاف ما إذا كاتبه على ثوب حيث لا يعتق بأداء ثوب لأنه يختلف اختلافاً فاحشاً ولو أدى قيمة الثوب لا يعتق إلا إذا علقه بأن قال إذا أديت إلي أ ألفاً فأنت حر فيعتق بأداء الثوب لصريح التعليق . وفي التتارخانية : ولو كاتبه على ثوب ولم يقل هروي أو غيره فهي فاسدة وفي الولوالجية : لو كاتبه على قيمة ثوب فهي فاسدة.
He (the author) said: 'And he does not decrease from the named amount, and is increased upon it.' This issue has no connection with the issue of wine; rather it is an independent issue. Its meaning: He made a kitabah with his slave on one thousand and his service forever, or on one thousand and a gift; the service forever and the gift are not valid as a substitute, so the contract is invalid. If he pays the thousand, he becomes free. If the thousand equals his value, the master has no claim on him; if his value is more, the master claims the excess; if the thousand is more than his value, he does not become free except by paying it. If he made a kitabah on one thousand and a pound of wine, he does not become free until he pays the thousand and the pound of wine. Thus in al-Muhit briefly. The commentator said: Because it is an invalid contract, so his value is obligatory upon him, whatever it may be, except that the master was not willing to free him for less than the named amount, so it is not decreased from it if his value is less than the named amount; and the slave is willing to have increase in order to attain the honor of freedom, so it is increased upon him if his value exceeds the named amount so that he attains honor. When he makes a kitabah on his value, he becomes free by paying it because it is the substitute in the invalid contract, whether it was mentioned or not; so it is possible to consider the meaning of freedom in it. The effect of ignorance (juhala) in the invalid contract is not to invalidate the contract, unlike when he makes a kitabah on a garment, where he does not become free by paying the garment because it varies greatly; and if he pays the value of the garment, he does not become free unless he conditionalizes it by saying 'If you pay me a thousand, you are free'; then he becomes free by paying the garment due to explicit suspension. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: If he makes a kitabah on a garment and does not say 'Herawi' or otherwise, it is invalid. In al-Walwalijiyyah: If he makes a kitabah on the value of a garment, it is invalid.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage addresses the matn phrase 'ولم ينقص عن المسمى وزيد عليه' (and does not decrease from the named amount and is increased upon it). The author clarifies that this is a separate ruling from the wine case. The rule applies to invalid kitabah where the consideration includes an unspecified service or gift. The contract is fasid because the service (khidmah) or gift (hadiyah) is not a valid substitute in exchange. When the slave pays the named amount (e.g., 1000 dirhams), he becomes free. However, the amount he must pay depends on his value (qimah): if his value is less than the named amount, he pays the named amount because the master did not agree to less; if his value is more, he pays the value (the excess over named) because the slave is willing to pay more for freedom. If the named amount is more than his value, he must pay the entire named amount to be free. The passage also gives an example of kitabah on a thousand plus a pound of wine: he must pay both. Then it contrasts with kitabah on a garment (thawb): if the garment is unspecified and varies greatly, the contract is invalid, and paying the garment or its value does not effect freedom unless the master explicitly suspends it on payment (ta'liq). The distinction is that in kitabah on a garment, the juhala (ignorance) is excessive, so the contract is void (batil), not just fasid. In kitabah on value (qimah nafsih), it is valid because the value is known or determinable.

Key Terms
المسمى (al-musamma): The named amount of money (e.g., 1000 dirhams) specified in the kitabah contract.
القيمة (al-qimah): The market value of the slave; in invalid contracts, the slave must pay the value of himself as compensation.
الخدمة (al-khidmah): Service; it is not a valid substitute in a contract because it is indefinite and not a fixed wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a mukatab's kitabah is on 1000 dirhams and his service forever, and his value is 800 dirhams, how much must he pay to be free?
A) 800 dirhams (his value)
B) 1000 dirhams (the named amount)
C) He cannot be free because the contract is invalid
D) 1000 dirhams plus the value of service

Key Takeaway
In an invalid kitabah, the slave pays the greater of the named amount or his value, ensuring the master's consent is honored and the slave can achieve freedom by paying more if needed.

Invalid Transactions of the Mukatab (Gift, Charity, Will, Guarantee)
Rulings on invalid transactions of the mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four transactions that are invalid for a mukatab and explain the reason for their invalidity.

Core Idea
The mukatab cannot give gifts or charity because his property is pledged for his kitaba.

A mukatab is a slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments. While under this contract, his property is effectively tied to the kitaba. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists four transactions that are invalid for the mukatab: gift, charity, will, and guarantee. Understanding these restrictions is essential to protect the rights of both the mukatab and his master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid transactions
Read the Arabic text and list the four transactions that are not permissible for the mukatab: hiba (gift), sadaqa (charity), wasiyya (will), and kafala (guarantee).
These are all gratuitous or non-commercial acts.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
Consider why these are invalid. The mukatab's property is pledged to the master for the kitaba; giving it away would harm the master's right to receive the payments.
3
Note the consequence after freedom
Observe that even if the mukatab later becomes free, the gift and charity are not validated; they are returned because they were void from the start.
This shows the original act was fasid (void).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تجوز هبة المكاتب وصدقته ووصيته وكفالته في الحال ولو أعتق ترد له الهبة والصدقة لأنها وقعت فاسدة ولو دفع مضاربة أو أخذ مالاً مضاربة جاز، ويجوز له شركة العنان لا المفاوضة
The gift, charity, will, and guarantee of the mukatab are not permissible in the present state. If he is freed, his gift and charity are returned to him because they occurred as invalid. If he gives or takes property as mudaraba, it is permissible. Partnership of 'inan is permissible for him, not mufawada.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage lists several transactions that are invalid for the mukatab while he remains a mukatab: gift (hiba), charity (sadaqa), will (wasiyya), and guarantee (kafala). If he later becomes free, these transactions are not validated; the gift and charity are returned to him because they were void from the start. In contrast, mudaraba (sleeping partnership) is valid for the mukatab, as is partnership of 'inan (limited partnership), but not mufawada (unlimited partnership). The reason is that mudaraba and 'inan partnership involve commercial activity which aligns with the purpose of kitaba, while the others do not.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift given without consideration.
الصدقة (al-ṣadaqa): Charity given freely.
الوصية (al-waṣiyya): A will or bequest.
الكفالة (al-kafāla): A guarantee or suretyship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The will is valid only for up to one-third of his estate.

Key Takeaway
The mukatab's non-commercial gifts, charities, wills, and guarantees are void and not rectified by eventual freedom.

Valid Transactions of the Mukatab (Mudaraba and Partnership)
Rulings on valid transactions of the mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two valid transactions for a mukatab and explain why they are permissible.

Core Idea
The mukatab can engage in mudaraba and 'inan partnership because they are commercial and help him earn for freedom.

While the mukatab cannot give away his property gratuitously, he may engage in profit-seeking ventures that support his goal of manumission. This second part of the passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that mudaraba (sleeping partnership) and shirkat al-'inan (limited partnership) are valid for the mukatab. Mudaraba involves one party providing capital and the other labor, while 'inan partnership involves both partners contributing capital and labor. However, mufawada (unlimited equal partnership) is not valid because it requires equal capacity and full authority, which the mukatab lacks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the valid transactions
Read the Arabic and note the transactions that are permissible: mudaraba (giving or taking capital for profit-sharing) and shirkat al-'inan (limited partnership).
These are commercial activities aimed at earning profit.
2
Understand why they are valid
Consider that these transactions are commercial and directly help the mukatab earn money to pay off his kitaba. They do not harm the master's rights but rather facilitate freedom.
Contrast with the invalid acts which are gratuitous.
3
Note the exception of mufawada
See that mufawada (unlimited partnership) is explicitly excluded. This is because mufawada requires equal capital, labor, and authority, which a mukatab cannot fully provide due to his restricted status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تجوز هبة المكاتب وصدقته ووصيته وكفالته في الحال ولو أعتق ترد له الهبة والصدقة لأنها وقعت فاسدة ولو دفع مضاربة أو أخذ مالاً مضاربة جاز، ويجوز له شركة العنان لا المفاوضة
The gift, charity, will, and guarantee of the mukatab are not permissible in the present state. If he is freed, his gift and charity are returned to him because they occurred as invalid. If he gives or takes property as mudaraba, it is permissible. Partnership of 'inan is permissible for him, not mufawada.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage lists several transactions that are invalid for the mukatab while he remains a mukatab: gift (hiba), charity (sadaqa), will (wasiyya), and guarantee (kafala). If he later becomes free, these transactions are not validated; the gift and charity are returned to him because they were void from the start. In contrast, mudaraba (sleeping partnership) is valid for the mukatab, as is partnership of 'inan (limited partnership), but not mufawada (unlimited partnership). The reason is that mudaraba and 'inan partnership involve commercial activity which aligns with the purpose of kitaba, while the others do not.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-muḍāraba): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profits shared as agreed.
شركة العنان (shirkat al-ʿinān): A partnership where each partner contributes both capital and labor, and profits are shared according to agreement.
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍa): A partnership with full equality in capital, labor, and authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mudaraba and shirkat al-'inan are valid commercial transactions for the mukatab, but mufawada is not because it requires full equality and capacity.

Mukatab Writing Kitabah for His Own Slave
Ruling with proof (qiyas) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mukatab may write kitabah for his own slave, explain the qiyas proof with sale, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Kitabah is a sale, and a mukatab can sell — so he can write kitabah for his slave.

The mukatab enjoys a special legal capacity in Hanafi fiqh, allowing him to engage in trade and contracts. One of the most debated issues is whether he can write a kitabah contract for his own slave. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary provides the ruling, its proof by analogy with sale, and the response to the Shafi'i objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a mukatab writing kitabah for his slave. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible (sahih). The matn states: 'وكتابة عبده يعني يملك المكاتب أن يكاتب عبده'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) on sale: the mukatab can sell his slave, so by analogy he can write kitabah, which is a type of sale.
The commentary explicitly compares: 'الكتابة عقد اكتساب للمال فيملكها كما يملك البيع'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The illa (effective cause) is that both sale and kitabah are contracts for acquiring wealth (tamlik al-mal). Kitabah is even more beneficial because it delays transfer of ownership until payment.
The commentator argues: 'البيع يزيل الملك بنفسه والكتابة لا تزيل إلا بعد وصول البدل'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the mukatab cannot write kitabah because kitabah leads to emancipation, and the mukatab is not entitled to emancipate for money.
Shafi'i's dalil: the contract does not have an equivalent (like sale) and it results in freedom.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes: kitabah is a sale from the slave himself, not an emancipation. The mukatab owns the right by virtue of owning the slave, not by seeking emancipation.
The commentator says: 'إنما ملكه على أن الكتابة بيع من نفس العبد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب/ باب ما يجوز للمكاتب أن يفعله وما لا يجوز
وكتابة عبده والولاء له إن أدى بعد عتقه وإلا لسيده لا التزوج بلا إذن والهبة لغيره يصح أن المفاوضة، ويجوز إقرار المكاتب بالدين والعين والاستيفاء لأنه لا بد للتجار منه. ولو أقر المكاتب على ولده المولود في الكتابة بجناية لم يجز إقراره لأنه إقرار على غيره، فإن مات الولد وترك مالاً كان ذلك لأبيه وأخر إقراره وصار هو الخصم في الجناية لأنه ظهر المقر له في حقه بإقراره، وكذا لو أقر على ولده بدين لم يجز فإن اكتسب الولد مالاً وأخذه الأب نفذ إقراره عليه في المال مكاتب أو مأذون في يده أمة ادعى رجل أنها أم ولده أو مكاتبته فصدقه المكاتب أو المأذون فيه جاز ويدفعها إليه، وكذلك إن كان معها ولد دفعه إليه لأن إقراره بالوديعة اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وكتابة عبده يعني يملك المكاتب أن يكاتب عبده لأن الكتابة عقد اكتساب للمال فيملكها كما يملك البيع، وقد يكون الكتابة أنفع من البيع إذ البيع يزيل الملك بنفسه والكتابة لا تزيل إلا بعد وصول البدل، فإذا جاز البيع فأولى أن تجوز الكتابة. وقال الشافعي : لا يملك لأن العقد لا يتضمن مثله ولأنه يؤول إلى العتق وليس له يعتق على مال. قلنا : إنما ملكه على أن الكتابة بيع من نفس العبد وإنما لا يملك الإعتاق على مال وتعليق العتق على أداء المال لأن فيه إثبات الحرية مقصودة. وفي الزيادات : رجل مجهول النسب اشترى عبداً فكاتبه فاشترى المكاتب أمة فكاتبها ثم أقر المولى الأعلى المجهول النسب أنه عبد للمكاتب فهي مكاتبة على حالها للمكاتب الأعلى والمكاتب الأعلى للمكاتب الأسفل لأنه يصح إقراره على نفسه بالرق لأن حريته لم تثبته بدليل إلا أنه غير مصدق في حق المكاتب لما فيه من إبطال حق المكاتب فبقي حراً في حق المكاتب مكاتباً للحر لا لعبد مكاتبه وهذا كمجهولة النسب إذا أقرت بالرق لإنسان لم يبطل نكاحها ويؤدي المكاتب الأعلى بدل الكتابة إلى المكاتب الأسفل لأن مجهول النسب لما أقر بالرق لها صار هو وجميع أكسابه مملوكاً لها وبدل الكتابة من جملة أكسابه ومتى صار مجهول النسب عبداً في حق هذا الحكم لا يبرأ المكاتب إلا بالأداء إلى المكاتبة والمكاتبة تؤدي مكاتبتها إلى المكاتب الأعلى. ثم المسألة لا تخلو إما أن يؤديا متعاقباً أو معاً فإن أديا متعاقباً فأيهما أدى أولاً إلى صاحبه عتق ولا يكون ولاؤه لأحد لأن ما عداه إما عبد أو مكاتب وهما ليسا من أهل الولاية، وأيما أدى آخراً عتق وولاؤه للأول لأنه لما صار حراً صار أهلاً للولاء وإن أديا معاً عتقا ولا ولاء لأحدهما على الآخر لأن عتق كل واحد منهما قرن بعتق صاحبه فلا يكون أحدهما أهلاً للولاء حال عتق صاحبه، وإن عجز أحدهما صار مملوكاً للآخر لأنه إن عجز المكاتب صار مملوكاً للمكاتبة لأنه من كسب مجهول النسب، وإن عجزت المكاتبة فقد صارت أمة للمتكاتب والمقر عبدهما فصارا جميعاً للمكاتب، وإن عجزا معاً عتقت المكاتبة وصار المجهول مع المكاتب عبدين لها لأن المكاتب أقر برقبته لمجهول النسب ومجهول النسب أقر برقبته وجميع أكسابه للمكاتبة فقد صار المكاتب مقراً ،برقبته للمكاتب والمكاتبة لما قبلت المكاتبة من المكاتب فقد أقرت برقبتها للمكاتب فقد اجتمع إقرارها وإقرار المكاتب سابق على إقرار مجهول النسب وآخر إلا قرارين ناسبخ لأولهما لأن للآخر رد الأول ولم يوجد الرد للثاني فصح فصار الاعتبار لإقرار مجهول النسب لأنه آخرهما، وهذا كرجل مجهول النسب أقر بأنه مملوك لعبد رجل وأقر مولى العبد وهو مجهول النسب أنه مملوك لهذا المقر فهما جميعاً مملوكان لعبد مجهول النسب أقر بالرق للمكاتبة والمولى للمكاتبة وهو المكاتب أقر للمكاتب بالرق لمجهول النسب صارا مملوكين للمكاتبة ا هـ مختصراً .
قال رحمه الله : ( والولاء له إن أدى بعد عتقه لأن الولاء لمن أعتق وعتيقة المكاتب الأول وهو أهل للولاء عند عتق الثاني وكان ملكه تاماً فيه عند ذلك فثبت له ضرورة. وفي شرح الطحاوي : وإن أديا معاً عتقا وثبت ولاؤهما من المولى. وفي الأصل: وإن عجز الأول ورد في الرق ولم يؤد الثاني مكاتبته بعد بقي الثاني مكاتباً على حاله، ونظيره العبد المأذون له إذا أذن لعبده في التجارة ثم حجر على الأول بقي الثاني يصير مملوكاً للمولى على الحقيقة، فلو أعتقه نفذ عتقه، ولو كان الأول لم يعجز ولكن مات قبل الأداء ولم يؤد الثاني مكاتبته أيضاً فهو على وجهين : إن ترك الأول مالاً كثيراً سوى ما على المكاتب الثاني وبه وفاء ببدل الكتابة وفي هذا الوجه لا تنفسخ كتابته فيؤدي كتابته ويحكم بحريته في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته وما بقي يكون لورثته الأحرار، وإن لم يكن له وارثه فلمولاه ويؤدي الثاني مكاتبته إلى وارث المكاتب الأول، وإذا أدى وعتق كان ولاؤه لابن المكاتب حيث يرث ورثته المذكور. الثاني إذا مات ولم يترك وفاء سوى ما ترك على المكاتب الثاني وهو لا يخلو من وجهين : إن كان مكاتبة الثاني أقل من مكاتبة الأول ففي هذا الوجه تنفسخ مكاتبة الأول ويكون عبداً ويبقى الثاني مكاتباً للمولى، وإن كان مكاتبة الثاني مثل مكاتبة الأول أو أكثر منه وهذا الوجه لا يخلو إما أن حل مكاتبه الثاني وقت موت الأول فتنفسخ كتابة الأول فيؤدي الثاني إلى المولى ويحكم بحرية الثاني للحال وبحرية الأول في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته وما بقي من مكاتبة الثاني تكون لورثة المكاتب الأول إن كان له وارث حر ويكون ولاء الثاني للمكاتب الأول لا لمولى المكاتب الأول، وإن حل المكاتب للثاني بعد موت المكاتب الأول إن كان له وارث وإن لم يطلب المولى الفسخ من القاضي حتى حلت فالجواب فيه كالجواب فيما إذا مات الأول وقد حل ما على الثاني، وإن طلب من القاضي الفسخ تفسخ كتابة الأول فظهر قول المؤلف لو قال أو عتقا معاً بأداء مكاتبتهما لكان أولى ليفيد أن الولاء له في الحالتين. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : إذا مات الأول وقد حل ما على الثاني وقد ترك وفاء إلا أنه دين على الناس ولم يخرج الدين حتى أدى الأسفل إلى الأعلى ينظر في الولاء والميراث إلى أدى الكتابة ا هـ . يوم وفي المحيط : فإن مات الأول عن ابن ولم يترك إلا ما على الثاني ومات الثاني وترك ولداً مولوداً في الكتابة يسعى فيما بقي على أبيه ويؤدي إلى المولى من مكاتبة الأول، فإن فضل
Book of the Mukatab / Chapter of what the mukatab may do and what he may not. He may write kitabah for his slave, and the wala' belongs to him if he (the mukatab) pays after his (the slave's) emancipation, otherwise to his master. He may not marry without permission, but gifting to others is valid as in a mutual partnership. It is permissible for the mukatab to acknowledge (iqrar) a debt, a specific item, and to receive payment, because he cannot do without trade. If the mukatab acknowledges a crime on behalf of his child born during the kitabah, his acknowledgment is not valid because it is an acknowledgment on behalf of another. If the child dies and leaves property, that belongs to his father, and his acknowledgment is postponed, and he becomes the opponent in the crime because the beneficiary appeared in his right through his acknowledgment. Similarly, if he acknowledges a debt on behalf of his child, it is not valid. If the child acquires wealth and the father takes it, his acknowledgment against him in the wealth is valid, whether the child is a mukatab or a ma'dhun. If a man claims that a female slave in his possession is his umm walad or his mukatabah, and the mukatab or ma'dhun confirms him, it is valid, and he hands her over to him. Likewise, if she has a child with her, he hands him over to him, because his acknowledgment is like a trust. End. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And his writing kitabah for his slave means that the mukatab has the right to write kitabah for his slave, because kitabah is a contract for acquiring wealth, so he owns it just as he owns sale. Sometimes kitabah is more beneficial than sale, because sale removes ownership by itself, while kitabah only removes it after receipt of the equivalent. So if sale is permitted, then kitabah is even more permitted. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not have that right because the contract does not entail its like, and because it leads to emancipation, and he does not have the right to emancipate for money. We said: He only owns it because kitabah is a sale from the slave himself, and he does not have the right to emancipate for money and to suspend emancipation on payment because that establishes freedom as the intended goal. In al-Ziyadat: A man of unknown lineage bought a slave and wrote kitabah for him; then the mukatab bought a female slave and wrote kitabah for her; then the higher master of unknown lineage acknowledged that he (the mukatab) is a slave of the (lower) mukatab. So she (the female slave) remains as a mukatabah for the higher mukatab, and the higher mukatab is for the lower mukatab, because his acknowledgment of slavery for himself is valid since his freedom was not proven by evidence, except that he is not believed against the (lower) mukatab due to annulling the right of the mukatab. So he remains free in relation to the (lower) mukatab, a mukatab for a free man, not for his slave's slave. This is like a woman of unknown lineage if she acknowledges slavery to a person; her marriage is not invalidated. The higher mukatab pays the kitabah price to the lower mukatab, because when the person of unknown lineage acknowledged slavery to her, he and all his acquisitions become her property, and the kitabah price is among his acquisitions. Whenever the person of unknown lineage becomes a slave in this ruling, the mukatab is not discharged except by payment to the mukatabah, and the mukatabah pays her kitabah to the higher mukatab. Then the issue is either they pay consecutively or together. If they pay consecutively, whichever pays first to the other becomes free, and his wala' does not belong to anyone because the other is either a slave or a mukatab, and they are not eligible for wala'. Whichever pays later becomes free and his wala' belongs to the first because when he became free he became eligible for wala'. If they pay together, both become free and neither has wala' over the other because the emancipation of each coincided with the other's, so neither is eligible for wala' at the moment of the other's emancipation. If one of them becomes incapable, he becomes the property of the other because if the mukatab becomes incapable, he becomes the property of the mukatabah because he is from the acquisition of the person of unknown lineage. If the mukatabah becomes incapable, she becomes a female slave of the mukatab (the other), and the one who acknowledged (the person of unknown lineage) is their slave, so both become the property of the mukatab. If both become incapable together, the mukatabah becomes free and the person of unknown lineage along with the mukatab become two slaves for her because the mukatab acknowledged his own servitude to the person of unknown lineage, and the person of unknown lineage acknowledged his servitude and all his acquisitions to the mukatabah. So the mukatab acknowledged his servitude to the mukatab and the mukatabah, by accepting the kitabah from the mukatab, acknowledged her servitude to the mukatab. Thus her acknowledgment and the mukatab's acknowledgment preceded the acknowledgment of the person of unknown lineage, and the latter abrogates the former because the latter can reject the former, and since no rejection occurred for the latter, it is valid. So the consideration is for the acknowledgment of the person of unknown lineage because it is the later. This is like a man of unknown lineage who acknowledged that he is the property of a man's slave, and the master of the slave (who is of unknown lineage) acknowledged that he is the property of this acknowledger; then both are owned by the slave of unknown lineage. So the person of unknown lineage acknowledged slavery to the mukatabah, and the master (the mukatab) acknowledged to the mukatab (the other) slavery to the person of unknown lineage; they both become owned by the mukatabah. End, summarized.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the wala' is for him if he pays after his emancipation, because wala' is for the one who emancipates, and his (the first mukatab's) freed slave (the second) is the first mukatab, and he is eligible for wala' at the time of the second's emancipation, and his ownership was complete in him at that time, so it is established necessarily. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If they both pay together, they become free and their wala' is established from the master. In al-Asl: If the first becomes incapable and returns to slavery, and the second has not yet paid his kitabah, the second remains a mukatab as before. Its analogy is the authorized slave: if he grants permission to his slave for trade and then the first is interdicted, the second remains, becoming the property of the master in reality. If he then emancipates him, his emancipation is effective. If the first did not become incapable but died before payment, and the second has not paid his kitabah either, then there are two aspects: If the first left much wealth apart from what is owed by the second mukatab and it suffices to pay the kitabah price, then in this case his kitabah is not dissolved; he (the first) pays his kitabah and is judged to be free in the last part of his life, and what remains goes to his free heirs. If he has no heir, it goes to his master. The second pays his kitabah to the heir of the first mukatab. When he pays and becomes free, his wala' is for the son of the mukatab, where his mentioned heirs inherit. Second: if he died without leaving sufficiency except what is owed by the second mukatab, this has two aspects: If the second's kitabah is less than the first's kitabah, then in this case the first's kitabah is dissolved and he becomes a slave, and the second remains a mukatab for the master. If the second's kitabah is equal to or greater than the first's kitabah, then this aspect is either the second's kitabah is due at the time of the first's death; then the first's kitabah is dissolved, the second pays to the master, and the second is judged to be free immediately, and the first is judged to be free in the last part of his life, and what remains of the second's kitabah goes to the heirs of the first mukatab if he has a free heir, and the wala' of the second is for the first mukatab, not for the master of the first mukatab. If the second's kitabah is due after the death of the first mukatab, if the first has an heir and the master does not request cancellation from the judge until it becomes due, then the answer is the same as the answer in the case where the first died and what was due from the second had become due. If he requests cancellation from the judge, the first's kitabah is dissolved. So the author's statement becomes clear: if he had said 'or both become free together by payment of their kitabahs' it would have been better to indicate that wala' belongs to him in both cases. In Nawadir Ibn Sami'ah from Muhammad: If the first died and what was due from the second had become due, and he left sufficiency except that it is a debt owed by people and the debt was not collected until the lower paid to the higher, then the wala' and inheritance are considered at the time of payment. End. And in al-Muhit: If the first died leaving a son and nothing except what is owed by the second, and the second died leaving a son born during kitabah, he (the son) strives for what remains on his father and pays to the master from the first's kitabah. Whatever remains...
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from the commentary of Kanz al-Daqa'iq by Imam Zayla'i (author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq). It addresses several rulings regarding the mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission). The matn states that a mukatab may write a kitabah for his own slave, and the wala' (clientage) goes to him if he pays after the slave's emancipation, otherwise to his master. The commentary explains that the mukatab owns the right to write kitabah because kitabah is a contract for acquiring wealth, similar to sale; in fact it is more beneficial because sale transfers ownership immediately while kitabah delays it until payment. The commentator then addresses Imam al-Shafi'i's objection that the mukatab cannot do this because kitabah leads to emancipation and he has no right to emancipate for money. The Hanafi response is that kitabah is essentially a sale from the slave himself, not an emancipation. The commentary then discusses the mukatab's ability to give gifts (ja'iz) and his acknowledgment (iqrar) of debts and property, which is valid due to necessity for trade. However, his acknowledgment on behalf of his child born during kitabah is invalid because it is an acknowledgment on another. A complex scenario involving a person of unknown lineage (majhul al-nasab) is given to illustrate the effect of multiple acknowledgments and kitabah contracts among slaves, showing how the later acknowledgment can abrogate the earlier. The passage then returns to the issue of wala' when both parties pay at different times or simultaneously, and the rulings when one becomes incapable or dies. The commentary draws from various sources including al-Ziyadat, Sharh al-Tahawi, al-Asl, and Nawadir Ibn Sami'ah, showing the depth of Hanafi fiqh in dealing with layered ownership and contractual relations among slaves. The main takeaway is that a mukatab has limited but significant transactional capacity, akin to a free man in trade, but with restrictions regarding marriage (requires permission) and acknowledgment affecting others.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master, whereby he pays a sum in installments and becomes free upon completion.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave pays a specified amount over time and becomes free upon payment.
ولاء (wala'): The right of inheritance and clientage that belongs to the emancipator of a slave.
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment or confession of a right or liability against oneself.
مجهول النسب (majhul al-nasab): A person of unknown lineage, presumed free until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a mukatab permitted to write kitabah for his own slave?
A) Because the mukatab has full legal capacity like a free person.
B) Because kitabah is analogous to sale, which the mukatab is permitted to do.
C) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) specifically allowed it.
D) Because the mukatab owns his own person and can do whatever he wants.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a mukatab to write kitabah for his own slave by analogy to sale, overruling the Shafi'i objection that it leads to emancipation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Iqrar and Multi-Level Kitabah with Majhul al-Nasab
Commentary expansion on iqrar and multi-level kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rules of iqrar (acknowledgment) by a mukatab and apply the Hanafi rulings to a complex scenario involving multiple levels of kitabah and a person of unknown lineage (majhul al-nasab).

Core Idea
A mukatab's iqrar is valid for himself but invalid on behalf of others; subsequent acknowledgments can abrogate earlier ones.

Beyond writing kitabah, the mukatab has capacity to make iqrar (acknowledgments) and engage in trade. However, his iqrar on behalf of his child born during kitabah is invalid. The commentary presents a complex hypothetical involving a majhul al-nasab and multiple kitabah contracts, illustrating how subsequent iqrar can override earlier ones, and how wala' and freedom are determined when multiple parties pay or fail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rules of iqrar for a mukatab
The matn states: the mukatab's iqrar of a debt, specific property, and receipt is valid because he needs it for trade. But iqrar on behalf of his child is invalid because it is iqrar on another.
Iqrar is a confession against oneself, so it binds only the one who acknowledges.
2
Understand the complex scenario
The commentary presents a chain: a majhul al-nasab (free by default) buys a slave and makes him a mukatab; that mukatab buys a female slave and makes her a mukatabah; then the majhul al-nasab acknowledges he is a slave of the lower mukatab.
This creates a cascade of ownership and kitabah.
3
Trace the effects of acknowledgment
The majhul al-nasab's acknowledgment is valid against himself (he becomes a slave), but it does not affect the right of the first mukatab (the lower one) because that would annul his rights. So he remains free in relation to the lower mukatab, but owned by the female slave? Actually it's complex: the acknowledgment makes him a slave of the mukatabah (female slave), and his acquisitions become hers.
The later acknowledgment abrogates the earlier because the later one can reject the earlier.
4
Determine wala' and freedom based on payment order
If both mukatabs pay consecutively, the first to pay becomes free and has no wala' because the other is still a slave. The later to pay becomes free and his wala' goes to the first. If they pay simultaneously, both are free and no wala'.
Wala' belongs to the one who first becomes free and is eligible.
5
Apply the ruling when one becomes incapable
If the higher mukatab fails, he becomes the property of the lower mukatabah; if the lower fails, she becomes his property. If both fail together, the mukatabah becomes free and the two men become her slaves, based on the later acknowledgment.
This shows how iqrar determines ownership in layered scenarios.
