Master's Option When a Slave/Mukatab Commits a Crime and Then Is Made Mukatab or Incapacitated
Explains the master's option when a slave/mukatab commits a crime and then is made mukatab or becomes incapacitated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's khiyar (option) when a slave who commits a crime is later made mukatab or becomes incapacitated, and explain the Hanafi qiyas that supports this ruling.

Core Idea
The master may hand over the slave or pay the lesser of value or damages when the obstacle to delivery is removed.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, al-Marghinani explains a nuanced ruling: when a slave commits a crime and the master, ignorant of the crime, makes him a mukātab (a slave working for his freedom), the master initially becomes liable for the slave's value because he prevented delivery. However, if the mukātab later becomes incapacitated, the obstacle to delivery is removed, and the master's option to either hand over the slave or pay the lesser of the slave's value or the crime's damages is restored. The same rule applies to a mukātab who commits a crime before judgment and then becomes incapacitated, analogized to the cases of mudabbar and umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial scenario and liability
A slave commits a crime. The master, unaware, makes him a mukātab. This prevents handing over the slave to the victim, so the master liable for the slave's value.
2
Recognize the temporary obstacle
The kitāba creates a barrier to delivery, but it is on the verge of removal (the mukātab may become free or incapacitated).
3
Determine effect of subsequent incapacity
When the mukātab becomes incapacitated, the obstacle is removed. The master now has the option (khiyar) to either hand over the slave or pay the lesser of the slave's value or the crime's damages.
4
Apply qiyas to analogous cases
The same reasoning applies to a mukātab who commits a crime and then becomes incapacitated, and to mudabbar and umm walad, where the master is liable for the lesser of value or damages because he is entitled to their earnings.
5
Note special rules for multiple crimes
If multiple crimes occur before any judgment, only one value is due from the master. If a crime occurs after a first judgment, it is treated independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وإن جنى عبد فكاتبه سيده جاهلاً بها فعجز دفع أو فدى) يعني المولي بالخيار إن شاء دفع العبد وإن شاء فداه بالأرش لأنه لو كاتبه وهو لا يعلم بالجناية لزمه قيمته لأنه لم يصر مختاراً للفداء بالمكاتبة من غير علم وقد امتنع الدفع بفعله من غير أن مختاراً للفداء فيجب عليه الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش كما إذا أعتقه أو دبره أو استولد الأمة أو باعه بعد ما جنى من غير علم بها إلا أن المانع من الدفع على شرف الزوال فلم ينتقل حق ولي الجناية من العبد إلى القيمة، فإذا عجز زال المانع فيتخير بين الدفع والفداء على القاعدة ا هـ : رحمه الله: (وكذا إن جنى مكاتب ولم يقض به فعجز) حكمه كالأول لأنه لما وجناية القن يخير فيها المولى بين الدفع والفداء على ما عرف وقبل أن يعجز يجب الأقل من الأرش لأن دفعه متعذر وهو أحق بكسبه من المولى وموجب الجناية عند تعذر الدفع يجب على من يكون له الكسب، ألا ترى أن جناية المدبر وأم الولد توجب على المولى الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش لما أنه أحق بكسبهما، ولو جنى جناية بعد الحكم عليه بالأولى فهي كالأولى. وإذا اجتمعت الجنايات في وقت قبل القضاء لم يلزمه إلا قيمة واحدة كذا في المبسوط. وفيه : وإذا جنى العبد المكاتب ثم عتق فهو على خياره، وإن عجز فالخيار للمولى، البحر الرائق / ج ۸/ ۸۴
He (author) said: (And if a slave commits a crime, and his master makes him a mukātab ignorant of it, then he becomes incapacitated, he gives (the slave) or ransoms) meaning the mawlā has the option: if he wishes, he hands over the slave, and if he wishes, he ransoms him with the damages. Because if he made him a mukātab while not knowing of the crime, the slave's value is due from him (the master), since he did not become free to choose ransom by the kitāba without knowledge. And paying (the slave) has been prevented by his own act, and he is not free to choose ransom, so the lesser of the slave's value or the damages is due from him. This is like the case if he freed him, or made him mudabbar, or had a child from a slave-girl, or sold him after the crime without knowledge. However, the obstacle to handing over is on the verge of removal, so the right of the guardian of the crime does not transfer from the slave to the value. So when he becomes incapacitated, the obstacle is removed, and he has the choice between handing over or ransom, according to the rule. End. He (author) said: (And likewise if a mukātab commits a crime and no judgment is passed against him, then he becomes incapacitated) his ruling is like the first. Because the crime of a slave (qinn) gives the master the option between handing over and ransom, as known. And before he becomes incapacitated, the lesser of the damages is due, because handing over is impossible and the master is more entitled to his earnings than the mawlā. And the liability for the crime when handing over is impossible falls upon the one who has the earnings. Do you not see that the crime of a mudabbar and umm walad imposes upon the master the lesser of his value or the damages, because he is more entitled to their earnings? And if he commits a crime after the first judgment, it is like the first. And if multiple crimes occur at the same time before judgment, only one value is due from him, as in al-Mabsūṭ. And in it: And if the slave-mukātab commits a crime and then becomes free, he retains his option; if he becomes incapacitated, the option goes to the master. Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, vol. 8, p. 84.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage contains two related rulings. First, if a slave commits a crime and his master makes him a mukatab without knowing about the crime, then later the mukatab becomes incapacitated, the master has a choice: either hand over the slave to the victim's guardian or pay the lesser of the slave's value or the crime's compensation. Initially, when the master made him a mukatab ignorantly, he is liable for the slave's value because he prevented handing over by his own act. However, that obstacle is temporary. Upon incapacity, the obstacle is removed, and the master's option of handing over returns. The second ruling is analogous: if a mukatab (who was already a mukatab) commits a crime before any judgment, and then becomes incapacitated, the master faces the same choice. The commentary analogizes to the cases of a mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and umm walad (slave-mother of master's child), where the master is liable for the lesser of value or damages. Finally, it notes that if the mukatab commits multiple crimes before judgment, only one value is due; if he commits a crime after a first judgment, it's treated independently.

Key Terms
جناية (jināyah): A crime that entails liability for damages (arash) or qiṣāṣ. Here, it refers to offenses against the person.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose freedom is deferred to the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to the master's child, gaining certain rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave commits a crime, then the master makes him a mukātab ignorantly, and then the mukātab becomes incapacitated, what are the master's options?
A) He must pay the full value of the slave.
B) He must hand over the slave without option.
C) He may choose to hand over the slave or pay the lesser of the slave's value or the damages.
D) He is not liable for the crime.

Key Takeaway
The master's option to hand over the slave or pay the lesser of value or damages is restored when the obstacle to delivery (kitāba) is removed by incapacity, with the ruling analogized to mudabbar and umm walad.

Manumitter Cannot Contract Wala with Another
States that a manumitter cannot contract wala with another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a manumitter cannot contract wala with another and explain why wala al-itaq is binding and exclusive.

Core Idea
Wala al-itaq is binding and exclusive; the manumitter cannot transfer or duplicate it.

You have learned that freeing a slave creates a bond of wala between the manumitter and the freed slave. But can the manumitter then contract a different wala with someone else? This ruling clarifies that wala al-itaq is a permanent bond that cannot be transferred or replaced. The commentary provides a rational proof based on inheritance precedence.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The matn clearly states: The manumitter does not have the right to contract wala with anyone else. This is a prohibition (haram).
The ruling is absolute — no exception.
2
Identify the underlying principle
Wala al-itaq is binding (lazim) and permanent. Once established, it cannot be rescinded or transferred. Therefore, entering another wala contract is impossible.
The term 'lazim' indicates an inseparable legal bond.
3
Examine the evidence
The commentary gives a rational proof: inheritance by wala al-itaq takes precedence over inheritance by wala al-muwalat. This shows that wala al-itaq is the stronger, primary bond.
The example of a deceased with two patrons illustrates this precedence.
4
Apply the principle to inheritance scenarios
If the manumitter (higher patron) dies before the client, only the manumitter's sons inherit the wala (not daughters), similar to wala al-itaq rules.
This detail from al-Mabsut reinforces the exclusivity of wala al-itaq.
5
Recognize the unanimity
This ruling is not disputed; it is agreed upon across madhabs. The manumitter cannot contract wala with another, period.
Cross-madhhab status: muttafaq alayh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وليس للمعتق أن يوالي أحداً) لأن ولاء العتاقة لازم لا يحتمل النقض بعد ثبوته فلا ينفسخ ولا ينعقد معه لأنه لا ينقل لأن الإرث بولاء العتاقة مقدم على الإرث بولاء الموالاة، ألا ترى أن شخصاً لو مات وترك مولى عتقه ومولى موالاته كان المال للمعتق. قال في المبسوط : ولو مات الأعلى ثم مات الأسفل فإنما يرثه المذكور من أولاد الأعلى دون الإناث على نحو ما بينا في ولاء العتاقة.
He, may God have mercy on him, said: (And the manumitter does not have the right to contract wala with anyone) because wala al-itaq is binding and does not admit rescission after it is established; thus it is not rescinded and no other wala can be contracted with him because it cannot be transferred. Indeed, inheritance by wala al-itaq takes precedence over inheritance by wala al-muwalat. Do you not see that if a person dies leaving a patron by manumission and a patron by contract, the wealth goes to the manumitter. He said in al-Mabsut: If the higher one dies then the lower one dies, only the mentioned sons of the higher one inherit from him, not the daughters, according to what we explained in wala al-itaq.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This is a clear ruling that the manumitter (mu'tiq) cannot enter into a wala al-muwalat contract with anyone because his wala is from manumission (wala al-itaq), which is binding (lazim) and cannot be broken. Therefore, it cannot be transferred or replaced. The commentary provides a rationale: in inheritance, wala al-itaq takes precedence over wala al-muwalat. If a deceased has both a patron by manumission and a patron by contract, the inheritance goes to the manumitter. Al-Mabsut adds details about inheritance when the higher patron dies before the lower client: only the sons (not daughters) of the higher patron inherit the wala, similar to rules in wala al-itaq.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The person who manumits a slave and thus holds wala al-itaq.
ولاء العتاقة (wala al-itaq): The bond of loyalty and inheritance established between the manumitter and freed slave.
ولاء الموالاة (wala al-muwalat): A contractual wala entered into by free persons for purposes of mutual inheritance.
لازم (lazim): Binding, irrevocable — a legal commitment that cannot be unilaterally terminated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can't a manumitter contract wala al-muwalat with another person?
A) Because wala al-muwalat is stronger
B) Because wala al-itaq is binding and cannot be transferred
C) Because he already has a contract
D) Because it is prohibited by Quran

Key Takeaway
Wala al-itaq is binding and exclusive; the manumitter cannot contract wala with another person.

Coerced Sale: Validity and the Dispute with Zufar
Ruling on coerced sale based on intent and dispute with Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of a coerced sale in Hanafi fiqh and evaluate the disagreement with Zufar over ownership transfer.

Core Idea
A coerced sale is corrupt not void; ownership transfers upon delivery, but the coerced person retains the option to rescind.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced case: a person forced to sell or buy. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the contract's essence and its description, leading to a surprisingly different outcome than absolute voidance. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the role of intent and the effect of subsequent conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and its basis
Note that a coerced sale is initially classified as fasid (corrupt) because the essence of the contract (ijab and qabul) is valid but a condition (free consent) is missing. Under what circumstances does the coerced person's statement change this ruling?
The key is whether the coerced person intended to create the sale (insha) or merely to inform falsely (ikhbar).
2
Locate the disagreement with Zufar
Zufar holds that a coerced sale is suspended (mawquf) and does not transfer ownership until ratified by the coerced person after the coercion is removed. Contrast this with Abu Hanifa's view.
The dispute hinges on whether the contract's validity in essence is sufficient for ownership to pass upon delivery.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof
Follow the reasoning: the pillars of sale (offer and acceptance) are present and linked to the subject matter, so the contract is legitimate in essence. The defect is only in the description (lack of consent). Therefore, ownership transfers upon delivery (qabd).
The proof is that the contract is structurally complete; the defect is removable by subsequent consent.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical difference: if the buyer disposes of the item in an irrevocable way (e.g., freeing a slave), is it valid? According to Abu Hanifa, yes, because ownership has already transferred. According to Zufar, no, because the sale is suspended.
This illustrates how a theoretical usul difference affects real transactions.
5
Apply the analogy to other corrupt sales
The author compares coerced sale to other corrupt sales (fasid). Consider how the Hanafi rule that fasid sales transfer ownership upon delivery applies here, with the right of rescission for the coerced party.
This qiyas strengthens the argument by showing consistency across the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما يكون فاسداً إذا قال المكره تلفظت بالبيع طبق ما أردت فإذا قال أردت الإخبار به كاذباً أو قال أردت إنشاء البيع فهو بيع صحيح لا خيار فيه ولا فساد أخذاً من التفصيل في حالة العتق. وقال زفر : لا يثبت به الملك لأنه موقوف ولنا أن ركن البيع وهو الإيجاب والقبول أهله مضافاً إلى محله فيكون مشروعاً بأصله غير مشروع بوصفه فيفيد الملك بالقبض حتى لو قبضه وتصرف فيه تصرفاً لا يحتمل النقض كالإعتاق والتدبير جاز تصرفه، وإنما لا تفسد بالإجارة لأن المفسد يرتفع بها وهو عدم الرضا فصار كسائر البياعات لفساده.
It is only corrupt if the coerced person says: 'I uttered the sale as per what I desired.' So if he says: 'I intended to inform falsely' or 'I intended to create the sale', then it is a valid sale with no option and no corruption, taken from the detailed ruling in the case of emancipation. And Zufar said: Ownership is not established by it because it is suspended. And for us: the pillar of sale, which is offer and acceptance, its parties are connected to its object, so it is legitimate in its essence but illegitimate in its description, so it confers ownership upon delivery, so that if he takes delivery and disposes of it in a way that does not admit of rescission, like emancipation and tadbir, his disposition is valid. And it is not invalidated by a condition of option because the invalidating factor is removed, which is lack of consent, so it becomes like other corrupted sales.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author discusses the ruling on a coerced sale (بيع مكره). The key is the coerced person's statement after the fact. If he says he uttered the sale according to his own desire (طبق ما أردت), the sale is corrupt (فاسد). If he says he intended to inform falsely (إخبار كاذب) or intended to create the sale (إنشاء البيع), then the sale is valid (صحيح) with no option to rescind and no corruption. This is derived from the detailed case of coerced emancipation. Then Zufar (Zufar ibn Hudhayl) holds that such a sale does not transfer ownership because it is suspended until ratification. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa's) is that the sale's pillars are present, so it is valid in essence but described as corrupt due to lack of consent. Therefore, it transfers ownership upon delivery (قبض). If the buyer then uses the item in an irrevocable way (like setting a slave free or making a tadbir), it is valid. The sale is not void due to a condition of option (الإجارة) because the impediment (lack of consent) is removed by such irrevocable acts. Thus it becomes like other corrupt sales (بياعات فاسدة) which are not void but have the right of rescission.

Key Terms
المكره (al-mukrah): The person who is compelled to enter into a contract against his will.
الإخبار (al-ikhbar): Informing; here, a false statement of sale without intent to create legal effect (ikhabar kadhib).
الإنشاء (al-insha'): Creation; the actual intent to form a contract by offer and acceptance.
الفاسد (al-fasid): Corrupt; a contract valid in essence but lacking a condition (like free consent).
الموقوف (al-mawquf): Suspended; a contract that does not take effect until a condition (like ratification) is fulfilled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling if a coerced buyer receives the purchased item and then emancipates a slave that was the subject of the sale?
A) The emancipation is valid because the sale transfers ownership upon delivery.
B) The emancipation is invalid because the sale is suspended.
C) The emancipation is valid only if the seller ratifies the sale.
D) The emancipation is invalid because the slave remains the seller's property.

Key Takeaway
A coerced sale is not void; it is corrupt (fasid) but ownership transfers upon delivery, and subsequent irrevocable acts confirm the contract's validity.

Sales Under Coercion: Ratification and Compensation
Explains rulings on sales under coercion, including multiple transactions, ratification, and compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for sales under coercion, distinguishing between the effects of ratification (ijāza) and compensation (ḍamān) on a chain of subsequent contracts.

Core Idea
Ratification validates all prior sales; compensation only validates subsequent sales unless paid by the first buyer.

When a sale is concluded under coercion, it is not absolutely void but voidable. The coerced party has the right to rescind, but can also choose to ratify or claim compensation. This lesson unpacks the intricate rules from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq on how these choices affect a series of transactions, the rights of subsequent buyers, and the liability of the coercer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of coercion and parties
Determine whether the seller, buyer, or both are coerced. The ruling changes accordingly. For a chain of sales, start from the coerced original party.
Coercion (ikrāh) must be unlawful and overwhelming (muljiʾ) to affect contracts in the Hanafi school.
2
State the default: the sale is fāsid (voidable)
A sale under coercion is not bāṭil but fāsid due to lack of free consent. It can become valid by subsequent ratification (ijāza) or by removal of coercion.
Until ratified, either party may rescind before taking possession.
3
Distinguish ratification (ijāza) from compensation (ḍamān)
Ratification removes the right to rescind and validates all prior contracts in the chain. Compensation does not remove the right to rescind but may validate subsequent contracts, depending on who pays.
If the coerced buyer compensates the first buyer, all sales become valid. If he compensates a later buyer, only sales after that buyer are valid.
4
Apply to a chain of multiple sales
Trace the effect: if the coerced buyer ratifies any contract, all prior contracts become valid because coercion ends. If he compensates the first buyer, all sales are valid. If he compensates a later buyer, only sales after that compensator are valid.
The first buyer's compensation establishes his ownership, making subsequent sales valid. Compensation by others does not retroactively validate earlier sales.
5
Handle special cases: perished goods, emancipation, and rescission rights
If the goods perish in the hands of a non-coerced buyer, he must pay the value. If the buyer is coerced, he is a trustee. Emancipation by a subsequent buyer before ratification invalidates later ratification. Rescission rights differ before and after possession.
The coerced seller can only rescind before possession; the coerced buyer can rescind after possession as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه
يضمن المكره وعلى أكل لحم خنزير وميتة ودم وشرب خمر يحبس أو ضرب أو قيد لم
أجاز الآخر العقد فنقض القاضي نفذ وألزم، وإن كان كلاهما مكرهاً أو مشروطاً له شرطاً فاسداً فلكل واحد منهما نقضه من غير قضاء ولا رضا لأنه قبل القبض لا يفيد شيئاً. ولو باع المشتري المكره من آخر باعه الثاني من آخر حتى تداولته الأيدي فله أن يفسخ العقود كلها وأي عقد جاز جازت العقود كلها إلا أنه لما أجاز بعض العقود فقد زال الإكراه وصار طائعاً راضياً فجاز العقد الأول فجازت العقود ويأخذ هو الثمن من المشتري الأول ولو لم يجز لكن ضمن، فإن ضمن الأول نفذ الكل بتضمينه، وإن ضمن غيره جازت البياعات التي بعده وبطل ما قبله والفرق بين الإجازة والتضمين أن البيع كان موجوداً والمانع من النفوذ حقه وقد زال بالإجازة، وأما إذا ضمن لم يكن مسقطاً حقه بخلاف ما إذا أجاز أحد بيوع الفضولي حيث لا يجوز إلا الذي أجازه المالك ولا يجوز ما قبله وما بعده لأن كل واحد منهم باع ملك غيره فلا يفيد الملك فعند الإجازة يملكه من أجيز شراؤه وتبطل البقية، فإن أعتق المشتري الثاني فللمكره أن يضمن أي الثلاث شاء لأن كل واحد منهم أحدث سبب الضمان بإزالة يده ملكه والمشتريان قبض كل واحد منهما ماله بغير إذنه. وفي الخانية : لو أعتق المشتري عن الآخر قبل إجازة البيع جاز العتق على الذي أعتقه، فإن أجاز البائع البيع الأول بعد ذلك لا يصح إجازته. وفي الخانية : لو أعتق المشتري الأخير أو كان له الخيار إن شاء ضمن المشتري الأول وإن شاء ضمن غيره فإن ضمن المشتري الأول جازت البياعات كلها، وإن ضمن غيرها يجوز كل بيع بعده ويبطل كل بيع كان قبله .اهـ. وفي قاضيخان : ولو كان البائع مكرهاً والمشتري غير مكره فقال المشتري بعد القبض نقضت البيع لا يصح، ولو قال قبل القبض نقضه، ولو كان المشتري مكرهاً والبائع غير مكره فلكل واحد منهما النقض قبل القبض ضح وبعد القبض يكون للمشتري دون البائع .
رحمه
قال رحمه الله : (وإن هلك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو غيره مكره والبائع مكره ضمن قيمته للبائع) لأنه قبضه بحكم عقد فاسد فكان مضموناً عليه بالقيمة. قيد بقوله «والمشتري غير مكره قال قاضيخان ولو كان المشتري مكرهاً دون البائع فهلك المشتري عنده من غير تعد منه يهلك أمانة .اهـ. ولو قال ضمن بدله كان أولى لأنه يشمل المثلي والقيمي. قال الله : ( وللمكره أن يضمن المكره لأنه آلة له فيما يرجع إلى الإتلاف وإن لم يكن له آلة في حق المتكلم لعدم الصلاحية لأن التكلم بلسان الغير لا يمكن فصار كأنه دفع مال البائع إلى المشتري فيضمن أيهما شاء كالغاصب وغاصب الغاصب، فإن ضمن المكره رجع المكره على المشتري بالقيمة لأنه بأداء الضمان ملكه فقام مقام المالك المكره فيكون مالكاً له من وجود السبب بالاستناد ولو ضمن المشتري ثبت ملك المشتري فيه ولا يرجع على المكره لأنه ملكه بالشراء والقبض غير أنه توقف نفوذه على سقوط حق المكره من الفسخ فإذا ضمنه قيمته وقت نفذ ملكه فيه كسائر البياعات الفاسدة.
Book of Coercion. The coerced person guarantees. And regarding eating pork, carrion, blood, and drinking wine, being imprisoned, beaten, or shackled, if the other party ratifies the contract, and the judge rescinds, it is enforced and binding. If both are coerced, or there is a void condition, each has the right to rescind without a court order or consent, because before taking possession it is of no effect. If the coerced buyer sold to another, and the second sold to another, until it passes through many hands, he may rescind all contracts. Whichever contract is ratified, all contracts are ratified, except that when he ratifies some contracts, coercion ceases and he becomes willing and content, so the first contract is valid and all subsequent contracts are valid, and he takes the price from the first buyer. If he does not ratify but compensates, if he compensates the first, everything is executed by his compensation; if he compensates another, the sales after him are valid and what came before is void. The difference between ratification and compensation is that the sale existed and the obstacle to its effect was his right, which has been removed by ratification. As for compensation, it does not waive his right, unlike the case when one ratifies a sale by a usurper (fuduli), where only the sale ratified by the owner is valid, and previous and subsequent sales are not, because each of them sold another's property, so ownership is not acquired. Upon ratification, the one for whose purchase it is ratified owns it, and the rest are void. If the second buyer emancipates [the slave], the coerced person may claim compensation from any of the three, because each of them created the cause of liability by removing his possession from his property, and the buyers each took their consideration without his permission. In al-Khaniyah: If the buyer emancipates from the other before ratification of the sale, the emancipation is valid on the one who emancipated; if the seller later ratifies the first sale, his ratification is not valid. In al-Khaniyah: If the last buyer emancipates, or he has an option, if he wishes he may claim compensation from the first buyer, or from another. If he claims from the first buyer, all sales are valid; if he claims from another, every sale after him is valid and every sale before him is void. In Qadikhan: If the seller is coerced and the buyer is not coerced, and the buyer says after taking possession, 'I rescind the sale,' it is not valid; if he says before taking possession, he rescinds it. If the buyer is coerced and the seller is not coerced, each has the right to rescind before taking possession; after taking possession, it is only for the buyer, not the seller. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If the sold item perishes in the hand of the buyer while he is not coerced and the seller is coerced, he guarantees its value to the seller,' because he took possession under a void contract, so it is guaranteed to him at its value. He restricted it by his saying 'and the buyer is not coerced.' Qadikhan said: If the buyer is coerced but not the seller, and the sold item perishes with him without transgression, it perishes as a trust. He said: If he said 'compensation in kind' it would be better, because it covers specific and fungible goods. Allah said: 'The coerced person has the right to claim compensation from the one who compelled him, because he is his instrument regarding destruction, even though he is not an instrument in terms of speech due to incapacity, because speaking with someone else's tongue is not possible. So it is as if he handed the seller's property to the buyer, so he may claim compensation from whichever he wishes, like the usurper and the usurper's usurper. If the coerced person compensates, he recovers the value from the buyer, because by paying compensation he owns it, so he stands in the place of the coerced owner, thus becoming owner of it retroactively from the cause. If the buyer compensates, his ownership in it is established, and he does not recover from the coerced person, because he owned it by purchase and taking possession, except that its effectiveness was contingent upon the removal of the coerced person's right to rescind. So when he compensates its value, his ownership takes effect in it like other voidable sales.'
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with sales under coercion. It begins by stating general rules: if one party is coerced and the other ratifies the contract, a judge may enforce it. If both are coerced or there is a void condition, each may rescind before possession. The text then discusses a chain of sales starting from a coerced buyer: the original coerced buyer can rescind all subsequent contracts, but if he ratifies any, the coercion ends and all earlier contracts become valid. Alternatively, he may claim compensation (daman) from any buyer in the chain. The key distinction is that ratification removes his right to rescind, while compensation does not, unless he chooses to compensate the first buyer, which validates all sales. The text cites al-Khaniyah and Qadikhan for specific cases, such as emancipation by a subsequent buyer. Finally, it addresses the case where the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand: if the buyer is not coerced, he must pay the value to the coerced seller; if the buyer is coerced, he is treated as a trustee and is not liable. The coerced party may claim compensation from either the coercer or the buyer, analogized to the case of a usurper and his usurper.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijāza): Ratification of a previously rescindable contract by the party entitled to rescind, thereby making it binding and effective.
التضمين (al-taḍmīn): Compensation or guarantee paid by one of the parties to the coerced owner for the loss of property, which does not necessarily waive the right to rescind.
البيع الفاسد (al-bayʿ al-fāsid): A voidable sale that lacks an essential condition (e.g., free will) but can become valid by subsequent ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a chain of sales starting from a coerced buyer, what happens if the coerced buyer compensates (ḍamana) the first buyer?
A) All sales become valid from the first to the last.
B) Only the sale between the coerced buyer and the first buyer becomes valid.
C) The coerced buyer loses all rights to the property.
D) None of the sales become valid.

Key Takeaway
A coerced sale is voidable (fāsid), and the coerced party has the choice to ratify (ijāza) or claim compensation (ḍamān), with different effects on subsequent transactions.

Transactions of Minors, Slaves, and the Insane: Capacity and Permission
Stating Hanafi rulings on validity of transactions by minors, slaves, and insane persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for transactions of non-discerning minors, discerning minors, slaves, and continuously insane persons, and explain the role of guardian permission and the concept of suspended contracts.

Core Idea
A non-discerning minor and a continuously insane person have no capacity; a discerning minor with understanding creates a suspended contract awaiting guardian approval.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi rulings on who can transact and under what conditions. You will learn why a minor's sale can sometimes be valid, why a slave needs his master's permission, and why the continuous insane cannot transact at all. The key distinction lies in understanding (aql) and permission (idhn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of incapacitated persons
Distinguish between a minor (non-discerning vs discerning), a slave, a continuously insane person, and an intermittently insane person. Note the different capacities.
Non-discerning minor and continuous insane: no discernment. Discerning minor: partial. Slave: full rational capacity but owned.
2
Determine the ruling for each category
For each category, decide if the transaction is absolutely invalid (batil), suspended (mawquf), or valid with permission. Use the text's reasoning: lack of intellect, risk of harm, benefit for guardian.
Non-discerning minor/continuous insane: batil. Discerning minor/slave: mawquf without permission, sahih with permission.
3
Analyze the concept of understanding (ya'qiluhu)
The text specifies that if the minor or slave 'understands' the transaction (knows profit/loss, intends benefit), the guardian can validate or annul. Explore what 'understanding' means in practice.
Understanding makes the contract suspended, not absolutely void.
4
Examine liability for destruction (itlaf)
Even incapacitated persons are liable for destroying property because actions are real and cannot be undone. They have a dhimma but payment is only due when able.
This applies to minors, slaves, and insane persons alike.
5
Review the intra-Hanafi dispute on consumption
When an interdicted person consumes the price in corruption, Abu Yusuf holds he must guarantee the like; Muhammad says no. Consider the practical consequences.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (فلا يصح تصرف صبي وعبد بلا إذن ولي وسيد لأن الصبي عديم العقل إذا كان غير مميز وإن كان مميزاً فعقله ناقص لعدم الاعتدال وهو البلوغ فيحتمل فيه الضرر فلا يجوز إلا إذا أذن له المولى فيصح حينئذ لترجح جانب المصلحة للمولى، فإذا أذن له بعد ذلك فيتصرف بأهليته إذا كان بالغاً عاقلاً وإن كان صغير فبأهلية الولي. وفي السراجية: للصغير الذي لا يعقل البيع إذا باع أو اشترى فأجاز الولي لم يصح ولو أذن القاضي للصبي بالتصرف صح تصرفه . قال رحمه الله : (ولا يصح تصرف المجنون المغلوب بحال) يعني لا يجوز تصرفه بحال ولو أجازه الولي لأن صحة العبارة بالتمييز وهو لا تمييز له فصار كبيع الطوطي، وإن كان يجن تارة ويفيق أخرى فهو في حال إفاقته كالعاقل والمعتوه كالصبي العاقل في تصرفاته
كتاب الإكراه باب الحجر
١٤٣
يجيزه الولي أو يفسخه وإن أتلفوا شيئاً ضمنوا ولا ينفذ إقرار الصبي والمجنون وينفذ
وفي رفع التكليف عنه وهو الناقص العقل، وقيل هو المدهوش من غير جنون. واختلفوا في تفسيره اختلافاً كثيراً وأحسن ما قيل فيه هو من كان قليل الفهم فاسد التدبير إلا أنه لا يضرب ولا يشتم كما يفعل المجنون .
قال رحمه الله : (ومن عقده منهم وهو يعقله يجيزه الولي أو يفسخه) يعني من عقد البيع والشراء من هؤلاء المحجورين وهو يعقله أي وهو يعقل أن البيع سالب والشراء جالب ويعلم الغبن الفاحش من اليسير ويقصد به تحصيل الربح والزيادة فالولي بالخيار إن شاء أجازه وإن شاء رده. فإن قيل : هذا في البيع يستقيم وأما في الشراء فلا يستقيم لأنه لا يتوقف بل ينفذ على المشتري. قلنا: إنما ينفذ على المشتري إذا وجد نفاذاً كشراء الفضولي وهنا لم يجد نفاذاً لعدم الأهلية أو لتضرر المولى فيتوقف الكل. قال في العناية : الأقوال موجودة حساً ومشاهدة فإما لها شروط في اعتبارها شرعاً لقصد دون العقل أجيب بوجهين: أحدهما الأقوال الموجودة حساً ليست عين مدلولها بل دلالات عليها ويمكن تخلف المدلول عن دليله فيمكن أن يجعل الموجود بمنزلة المعدوم بخلاف الأفعال فإن الموجود منها هو عينها، فبعد ما وجدت لا يمكن أن تجعل غير موجودة. الثاني القول قد يقع صدقاً وكذباً ويقع جداً وهزلاً فلا بد القصد بخلاف الفعل قال : فإن قيل قوله تصرف صبي وعبد الخ» يفيد أن عقدهما لا من ينعقد وقوله «ومن عقد منهم وهو يعقله يجيزه الولي أو يفسخ يفيد أن ينعقد موقوفاً وبينهما منافاة، فالجواب أن المراد بقوله لا يصح لا ينفذ وهو شائع في عبارة الفقهاء. فإن قيل: كان يمكنه أن يقول ومن عقد منهما بلفظ التثنية دون الجمع يعني الصبي والعبد. قلنا : فهم من قوله المغلوب غير المغلوب الذي بمنزلة الصبي والعبد فلذا عبر بلفظ الجمع. قال في العناية : أراد الصبي والمجنون الذي هو يجن ويفيق فإنه بمنزلة الصبي. قال ابن فرشته : الولي هو القاضي والولي الذي يلي التجارة في مال الصبي كالأب والجد والوصي ولا يجوز بإذن العم والأم والأخ ا هـ. وإذا رفع الأمر إلى القاضي لا يخلو إما أن يكون الثمن قائماً أو هالكاً، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون بيع رغبة أو غبينة وإذا رد المبيع والثمن قائم في يده رده، وإن كان المحجور استهلك الثمن ينكر إن استهلكه في النفقة وما يجوز له فإن القاضي يعطي الدافع مثله، وإن استهلكه فيما لا يحتاج إليه فإن صرفه في وجوه الفساد يضمن المحجور مثله عند الثاني، وعند محمد لا يضمن ؛ كذا في التتارخانية والظاهر أن الولي إذا علم بالبيع كالقاضي . قال رحمه الله : (وإن أتلفوا شيئاً ضمنوا لأنهم غير محجور عليهم في الأفعال إذ لا يمكن أن يجعل القتل غير القتل والقطع غير القطع فاعتبر في حقه فثبت عليه موجبه لتحقق السبب ووجود أهلية الوجوب وهي الذمة لأن الإنسان يولد وله ذمة صالحة لوجوب الحق إلا أنه لا يطالب بالأداء إلا عند القدرة كالمعسر لا يطالب بالدين إلا إذا أيسر وكالنائم لا يطالب
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'So the disposition of a minor and a slave is not valid without the permission of a guardian and master because the minor lacks intellect if he is non-discerning, and if he is discerning, his intellect is deficient due to lack of maturity, which is puberty; thus harm is probable, so it is not permissible unless the guardian permits, in which case it becomes valid because the benefit side preponderates for the guardian. Then if he permits afterward, he acts with his own capacity if he is mature and sane, but if he is young, he acts with the capacity of the guardian. In al-Sirajiyya: For a young child who does not understand sale, if he sells or buys and the guardian permits, it is not valid. But if the judge permits the child to act, his action is valid.' He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'The disposition of the continuously insane is not valid under any circumstance,' meaning his action is not permissible under any circumstance even if the guardian permits it, because the validity of expression is based on discernment, and he has no discernment, so it is like the sale of a parrot. And if he is insane sometimes and lucid at other times, then in his lucid state he is like a sane person. And the ma'tuh (mentally deficient) is like a discerning minor in his actions. The guardian may validate it or annul it. If they destroy something, they are liable. The acknowledgment of a minor and an insane person is not valid, but valid for... [cut off]. And regarding the removal of responsibility from him, he is deficient in intellect; it is said he is bewildered without insanity. They differed greatly in its interpretation, and the best said about it is that he is one of little understanding and corrupt management except that he does not hit or insult as the insane does. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Whoever among them contracts while understanding it, the guardian may validate it or annul it.' Meaning whoever among these interdicted persons makes a contract of sale or purchase while understanding it, i.e., while he understands that sale takes away and purchase brings, and knows excessive fraud from slight, and intends thereby to gain profit and increase, then the guardian has the option: if he wishes, he validates it; if he wishes, he rejects it. If it is said: this works for sale, but for purchase it does not, because purchase is not suspended but rather binding on the buyer. We say: it only binds the buyer if it has binding force, like the purchase of a stranger (fuduli), but here it has no binding force due to lack of capacity or due to harm to the guardian, so everything is suspended. In al-‘Inaya: statements exist sensibly and observably, but they have conditions for their validity in Shariah, including intent, not just intellect. Two answers: first, statements that exist sensibly are not the same as their meaning but are indications of it, and the meaning can be absent from its indicator, so the existing can be treated as non-existent, unlike actions, for the existing action is the very act itself; once it exists, it cannot be made non-existent. Second, a statement can be true or false, serious or jesting, so intent is necessary, unlike action. He said: If it is said: his statement 'the disposition of a minor and a slave' indicates that their contract does not occur at all, while his statement 'whoever among them contracts while understanding it, the guardian may validate it or annul it' indicates that it occurs as suspended, and there is contradiction between them. The answer: what is meant by 'not valid' is 'not executable' (la yanfudh), which is common in the terminology of jurists. If it is said: he could have said 'whoever among them contracts' using the dual form (meaning the minor and slave). We say: from his statement 'the continuously insane' he understands the non-continuously insane, who is like the minor and slave, so he used the plural. In al-‘Inaya: he meant the minor and the insane person who is sometimes insane and sometimes lucid, for he is like the minor. Ibn Firishtah said: The guardian is the judge and the guardian who manages the property of the minor, like the father, grandfather, and executor; permission from the paternal uncle, mother, or brother is not valid. End quote. When the matter is referred to the judge, either the price is still in existence or consumed, and either it is a sale at desire or with deception. If the sold item is returned and the price is still in his hand, he returns it. If the interdicted person consumed the price: if he consumed it in expenses and what is permissible for him, then the judge gives the payer the like thereof. If he consumed it in what he does not need, such as spending on corruption, then the interdicted person guarantees its like according to the second (Abu Yusuf), but according to Muhammad he does not guarantee; thus in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And it appears that if the guardian knows of the sale, he is like the judge. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if they destroy something, they are liable because they are not interdicted in actions, since it is impossible to make killing not killing and cutting not cutting; so it is considered regarding him, so its consequence is established due to the realization of the cause and the existence of the capacity for obligation, which is the dhimma (legal personality). For a human is born with a dhimma fit for the obligation of rights, except that he is not demanded with payment except when able, like an insolvent debtor is not demanded with the debt unless he becomes wealthy, and like a sleeping person is not demanded.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof commentary on the validity of transactions by minors, slaves, and the insane, from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The first ruling: the disposition of a minor or a slave is not valid without guardian or master permission. The reason given: a non-discerning minor lacks intellect entirely; a discerning minor has incomplete intellect (lack of puberty); thus there is risk of harm. With permission, the transaction becomes valid because the benefit outweighs. The minor who cannot understand sale (non-discerning) cannot have his transaction validated even by guardian. However, if the judge permits the minor to act, it is valid. Second ruling: the continuously insane person's transaction is invalid under any circumstance, even with guardian permission, because he lacks discernment (like a parrot's sale). But if he has intermittent insanity, during lucid periods he is like a sane person. The ma'tuh (mentally deficient) is treated like a discerning minor. Third ruling: those among the interdicted who contract while understanding (i.e., they comprehend the benefit/harm, know fair price, intend profit) produce a suspended contract. The guardian may validate or nullify it. The text addresses an objection about purchase not being suspensible: it becomes suspended due to lack of capacity or harm to the guardian. Fourth ruling: if they destroy property, they are liable because they are not interdicted in actions (since actions are real and cannot be undone). They have a dhimma for obligations, but payment is only due when they are able (like an insolvent or sleeping person). The text also includes intra-Hanafi disputes: regarding the guarantee when the interdicted person consumes the price in corruption: Abu Yusuf says he guarantees, Muhammad says no. The guardian's definition: the judge and the guardian managing the minor's property (father, grandfather, executor), not relatives like uncle, mother, brother.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor, one who has not reached puberty. Divided into discerning (mumayyiz) and non-discerning.
عبد (abd): A slave; owned by another.
مجنون مغلوب (majnun maghlub): A continuously insane person, whose insanity overcomes him at all times.
يعقله (ya'qiluhu): He understands it; i.e., comprehends the nature and consequences of the transaction.
ولي (wali): Guardian; the person authorized to manage the affairs of the minor or interdicted person.
إتلاف (itlaf): Destruction of property; causing loss.
ذمة (dhimma): Legal personality; capacity for obligations and rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A discerning minor (mumayyiz) sells a book without his guardian's permission. The sale is:
A) Valid and binding
B) Absolutely void
C) Suspended (mawquf) pending guardian approval
D) Valid only if the price is fair

Key Takeaway
The validity of a transaction by a minor or slave hinges on three factors: capacity (discerning/non-discerning), permission (guardian/master), and understanding (for suspended contracts).

Contracts of Interdicted Persons: Suspended Pending Guardian's Approval
Commentary on matn regarding contracts of interdicted persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on contracts of minors and interdicted persons, explain why they are suspended (mawquf), and identify the guardian's options.

Core Idea
A contract by a minor or weak-minded person is valid but suspended; the guardian may approve or cancel it.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a nuanced Hanafi position: contracts of interdicted persons (minors, insane, weak-minded) are not automatically void. Rather, if the person is sane at the time of contract, the contract is suspended (mawquf), giving the guardian a choice to authorize or cancel. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, including a qiyas (analogy) to the fuduli transaction, and addresses objections about differences between sale and purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the class of contracting party
Determine whether the person is a minor, insane (who may have lucid intervals), or weak-minded (safih). The ruling depends on the capacity of the person.
The weak-minded (safih) is not insane but lacks judgment; he is interdicted.
2
Assess sanity at the time of contract
The contract is considered only if the person 'ya'qiluhu' (is sane) at that moment, meaning he understands that sale divests and purchase acquires, and can distinguish excessive fraud.
If not sane, the contract is void (batil), not suspended.
3
Classify the contract as suspended (mawquf)
When the person is sane, the contract is valid in itself but its effectiveness is suspended pending the guardian's approval (ijaza) or rejection (faskh).
The guardian is the judge, father, grandfather, or executor (wasi).
4
Address objections via qiyas and linguistic distinction
An objection arises: purchase by a minor should be effective immediately, unlike sale. The commentary rebuts using qiyas to the fuduli transaction (unauthorized agent): purchase also lacks effect due to lack of capacity. Additionally, speech is treated differently from actions—speech can be disregarded as it is a sign that may not reflect true intent.
This shows the Hanafi use of rational proof (dalil aqli) and linguistic analysis.
5
Apply consequences if price is consumed
If the interdicted person consumes the price, the guardian's role depends on whether consumption was necessary or wasteful. An intra-Hanafi difference exists: Abu Yusuf holds the person guarantees if consumed in corruption; Muhammad holds no guarantee.
This uncertainty does not affect the core suspended ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجيزه الولي أو يفسخه. وفي رفع التكليف عنه وهو الناقص العقل، وقيل هو المدهوش من غير جنون. واختلفوا في تفسيره اختلافاً كثيراً وأحسن ما قيل فيه هو من كان قليل الفهم فاسد التدبير إلا أنه لا يضرب ولا يشتم كما يفعل المجنون. قال رحمه الله : (ومن عقده منهم وهو يعقله يجيزه الولي أو يفسخه) يعني من عقد البيع والشراء من هؤلاء المحجورين وهو يعقله أي وهو يعقل أن البيع سالب والشراء جالب ويعلم الغبن الفاحش من اليسير ويقصد به تحصيل الربح والزيادة فالولي بالخيار إن شاء أجازه وإن شاء رده. فإن قيل : هذا في البيع يستقيم وأما في الشراء فلا يستقيم لأنه لا يتوقف بل ينفذ على المشتري. قلنا: إنما ينفذ على المشتري إذا وجد نفاذاً كشراء الفضولي وهنا لم يجد نفاذاً لعدم الأهلية أو لتضرر المولى فيتوقف الكل. قال في العناية : الأقوال موجودة حساً ومشاهدة فإما لها شروط في اعتبارها شرعاً لقصد دون العقل أجيب بوجهين: أحدهما الأقوال الموجودة حساً ليست عين مدلولها بل دلالات عليها ويمكن تخلف المدلول عن دليله فيمكن أن يجعل الموجود بمنزلة المعدوم بخلاف الأفعال فإن الموجود منها هو عينها، فبعد ما وجدت لا يمكن أن تجعل غير موجودة. الثاني القول قد يقع صدقاً وكذباً ويقع جداً وهزلاً فلا بد القصد بخلاف الفعل قال : فإن قيل قوله تصرف صبي وعبد الخ» يفيد أن عقدهما لا من ينعقد وقوله «ومن عقد منهم وهو يعقله يجيزه الولي أو يفسخ يفيد أن ينعقد موقوفاً وبينهما منافاة، فالجواب أن المراد بقوله لا يصح لا ينفذ وهو شائع في عبارة الفقهاء. فإن قيل: كان يمكنه أن يقول ومن عقد منهما بلفظ التثنية دون الجمع يعني الصبي والعبد. قلنا : فهم من قوله المغلوب غير المغلوب الذي بمنزلة الصبي والعبد فلذا عبر بلفظ الجمع. قال في العناية : أراد الصبي والمجنون الذي هو يجن ويفيق فإنه بمنزلة الصبي. قال ابن فرشته : الولي هو القاضي والولي الذي يلي التجارة في مال الصبي كالأب والجد والوصي ولا يجوز بإذن العم والأم والأخ ا هـ. وإذا رفع الأمر إلى القاضي لا يخلو إما أن يكون الثمن قائماً أو هالكاً، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون بيع رغبة أو غبينة وإذا رد المبيع والثمن قائم في يده رده، وإن كان المحجور استهلك الثمن ينكر إن استهلكه في النفقة وما يجوز له فإن القاضي يعطي الدافع مثله، وإن استهلكه فيما لا يحتاج إليه فإن صرفه في وجوه الفساد يضمن المحجور مثله عند الثاني، وعند محمد لا يضمن؛ كذا في التتارخانية والظاهر أن الولي إذا علم بالبيع كالقاضي.
He (the guardian) may authorize it or cancel it. And regarding the removal of obligation from him (the person under interdiction), he is the one deficient in intellect. Some say he is the bewildered without insanity. They differed greatly in its interpretation. The best said is that he is one of little understanding and poor judgment, but he does not strike or abuse as the insane do. He (the author) said: (And whoever among them makes a contract while sane, the guardian may authorize or cancel it) meaning whoever conducts a sale or purchase from among these interdicted persons while sane, i.e., he understands that selling takes away and buying brings, and he knows excessive deception from slight, and intends profit and increase. The guardian has the choice to authorize or reject. If it is said: This is valid for sale, but for purchase it is not valid because it is not suspended but rather executed against the buyer. We say: It is only executed against the buyer if it had effect, like the purchase of a fuduli. Here it has no effect due to lack of capacity or harm to the guardian, so all is suspended. Al-Inayah said: Utterances are perceptible, but they have conditions for their consideration legally, requiring intention not mere intellect. It was answered with two points: First, utterances are not the same as their meanings but are indications; the meaning can lag behind. Thus the existing can be treated as nonexistent, unlike actions which are identical to their existence—once they occur they cannot be made nonexistent. Second, speech can be truthful or false, serious or jest, so intention is needed, unlike action. He said: If it is said that his statement 'the transaction of a child and slave is not valid' indicates their contract does not conclude, and his statement 'whoever among them contracts while sane, the guardian may authorize or cancel' indicates it is suspended, there is contradiction. The answer is that by 'not valid' he means 'not effective', which is common in jurists' language. If it is said: He could have said 'whoever of them contracts' in dual form referring to the child and slave. We say: He included the one overcome (by deficiency) not the one not overcome, and the one like the child and slave, so he used plural. Al-Inayah said: He meant the child and the insane person who alternates between sanity and insanity, for that person is like the child. Ibn Farishtah said: The guardian is the judge and the guardian who manages the child's wealth like father, grandfather, and executor; it is not permissible with permission of uncle, mother, brother. When the matter is raised to the judge, either the price remains or is consumed, and either it is a sale of desire or of duress. If he returns the sold item and the price remains in his hand, he returns it. If the interdicted person consumed the price, it is investigated: if consumed in necessary expenses, the judge gives the payer the equivalent; if consumed in unnecessary things like corruption, the interdicted person guarantees the equivalent according to the second, while Muhammad says no guarantee. Likewise in al-Tatarhaniyyah, and apparently the guardian if aware of the sale is like the judge.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding contracts of interdicted persons. The author first clarifies the meaning of 'mubligh' (one who is overcome) or 'saifi' (weak-minded), the person who is deficient in intellect but not insane. Then he explains that when such a person makes a contract while sane, the guardian has the option to approve or cancel it. The text then addresses objections: first, that purchase by a child is not suspended but effective, but the answer is that it lacks effect due to lack of capacity or harm, analogizing to the fuduli (unauthorized agent). Second, a distinction is made between speech and actions: speech can be treated as non-existent because it is a sign that may not correspond to reality, while actions are physical and cannot be undone. The commentary resolves the apparent contradiction between the matn statements about 'not valid' and 'suspended' by explaining that 'not valid' means 'not effective'. It then defines who the guardian is (judge, father, grandfather, executor) and discusses the judge's role when the price is consumed. It mentions an intra-madhhab difference: according to the 'second' (probably Abu Yusuf), the interdicted person guarantees if he consumes the price in forbidden things, while according to Muhammad, he does not. The authoritative Ottoman work al-Tatarhaniyyah is cited.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The guardian who oversees the affairs of the interdicted person, including the judge, father, grandfather, and executor of the will (waṣī).
يعقله (ya'qiluhu): The person is sane and cognizant of the nature of the transaction (that sale is divestment and purchase is acquisition) and capable of distinguishing excessive fraud from minor fraud.
يتوقف (yatawaqqaf): The contract is suspended (mawquf) pending approval or rejection by the guardian.
السفيه (al-safih): A weak-minded person who lacks proper judgment and is prone to waste, but is not insane (does not strike or shout).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) of a contract conducted by a minor who is sane under Hanafi law?
A) The contract is void (batil) and cannot be validated.
B) The contract is suspended (mawquf) and the guardian may approve or cancel it.
C) The contract is fully effective and binding.
D) The contract is considered a joke (hazl) and has no effect.

Key Takeaway
A contract by a minor or weak-minded person is not automatically void; it is suspended pending the guardian's approval, with the condition that the person was sane at the time of contract.

Three Differences Between Hajr due to Safah and Hajr due to Debt
Comparison of two types of hajr in al-Asl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three differences between interdiction due to safah and interdiction due to debt, and apply the rulings on iqrar and sa'yah in specific scenarios.

Core Idea
The purpose of interdiction determines its effect: safah protects the person, debt protects creditors.

This passage from al-Asl lays out three key differences between two types of legal interdiction (hajr): one due to foolishness (safah) and one due to debt. Understanding these differences is crucial for correctly applying rulings on manumission, sa'yah, and acknowledgment of debts. The first difference is the rationale, the second concerns sa'yah recovery, and the third concerns the validity of iqrar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of interdiction
Recognise that the passage compares hajr due to safah and hajr due to debt. Note that the first is for the person's own protection, the second for creditors.
Hajr due to safah is a form of protection for the individual, while hajr due to debt is a mechanism to secure creditors' rights.
2
Analyse the second difference: sa'yah after manumission
For the safih: manumission is invalid, so after sa'yah payment he cannot recover from the master. For the bankrupt: manumission is valid, so after payment he can recover from the master.
The difference stems from the validity of the manumission act itself.
3
Analyse the third difference: iqrar (acknowledgment)
The safih's iqrar is never valid, even after interdiction ends. The debtor's iqrar is only invalid during interdiction for existing wealth, but valid after, and valid for new wealth even during interdiction.
The safih is considered inherently unreliable, while the debtor's reliability returns after the creditors' claims are resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الأصل: الحجر بسبب السفه يقارن الحجر بالدين من ثلاثة وجوه الأول أن الحجر على السفيه لمعنى في ذاته أما الحجر بسبب الدين فلحق الغرماء. الثاني المحجور عليه بسبب السفه إذا أعتق عبداً ووجب عليه السعاية فإذا أدى لا يرجع به على المولى بعد زوال الحجر والمقضي عليه بالإفلاس إذا أعتق عبداً وبما في يده وجبت عليه السعاية، فإذا أدى يرجع بما أدى على المولى بعد زوال الحجر الثالث المحجور عليه بالدين يزول إقراره بعد زوال الحجر، وكذا حال قيام الحجر فيما يحدث في المال والمحجور عليه بالسفه لا يجوز إقراره لا في حال الحجر ولا بعد زوال الحجر لا في المال القائم ولا الحادث.
And in al-Asl: Interdiction due to safah is compared with interdiction due to debt in three aspects: First, interdiction of the safih is for a meaning in himself, while interdiction due to debt is for the right of the creditors. Second, the one interdicted due to safah, if he manumits a slave and then must perform sa'yah (working to pay the value), and he pays it, he does not recover it from the master after the removal of interdiction. The one adjudged bankrupt (for debt) if he manumits a slave while having sufficient property, sa'yah is obligatory, and when he pays, he recovers what he paid from the master after the removal of interdiction. Third, the one interdicted due to debt: his acknowledgment (iqrar) is nullified after the removal of interdiction, and likewise during interdiction concerning newly acquired wealth. But the one interdicted due to safah: his acknowledgment is not valid either during interdiction or after its removal, whether concerning existing or newly acquired wealth.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage from al-Asl (a foundational Hanafi text) lists three differences between interdiction due to safah (foolishness) and interdiction due to debt (insolvency). The first difference is the underlying rationale: safah-based interdiction is for the individual's own protection, while debt-based interdiction is to protect creditors. The second difference concerns manumission and sa'yah (the slave working to pay his value). In safah, if the safih manumits a slave and then performs sa'yah, he cannot recover the amount paid from the master after the interdiction ends, because the manumission was invalid and the slave's sa'yah is considered a fresh acquisition. In debt interdiction, the bankrupt's manumission is valid, and when he pays the sa'yah, he can recover it from the master after the interdiction ends. The third difference is about iqrar (acknowledgment of debt or liability). For the safih, his iqrar is never valid—neither during nor after interdiction—because he is inherently untrustworthy in financial matters. For the debtor, his iqrar is only nullified during interdiction for existing wealth, but after interdiction ends, his iqrar regarding that wealth becomes effective again, and during interdiction, iqrar about newly acquired wealth is valid.

Key Terms
السعاية (sa'yah): The obligation of a slave to work to pay his value to the master when he is partially manumitted or manumitted by an interdicted person.
الإقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment of debt or liability made by a person; its validity is affected by interdiction.
الغرماء (ghurama): Creditors to whom debts are owed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true regarding iqrar (acknowledgment) for a person interdicted due to safah?
A) Valid only during interdiction
B) Valid only after interdiction
C) Never valid, regardless of time or wealth
D) Valid for newly acquired wealth only

Key Takeaway
The safih's financial acts are fundamentally invalid; the debtor's acts are temporarily restricted for creditors.

Validity of Safih's Sale Before Qadi's Interdiction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on safih's sale validity before qadi's interdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding the validity of a safih's sale before a qadi's interdiction and identify the illa that drives each position.

Core Idea
A safih's sale before judicial interdiction is valid according to Abu Hanifa but invalid according to Muhammad, depending on whether safah is a self-evident cause.

In Hanafi fiqh, a safih is a person who spends wealth foolishly. The key question is whether a sa fih's sale is valid before a qadi formally imposes interdiction (hajr). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree on the role of judicial declaration versus the underlying cause (illah) of safah. This lesson unpacks their arguments and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the precise legal question: Is a sale by a safih valid before a qadi’s interdiction order?
2
Identify each position
Abu Hanifa holds the sale is valid; Muhammad holds it is invalid. Understand the core difference.
Muhammad is one of the two Sahibayn, so this is intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyse the proofs
Abu Hanifa: safah signs are ambiguous, so default capacity remains. Muhammad: safah is a clear cause (illah) that triggers interdiction automatically, analogous to insanity or minority.
Notice the qiyas: Muhammad analogizes safih to minor/insane; Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on certainty.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The text provides Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: the cause is not self-evident; it requires judicial investigation.
This is a key usul point: when the illah is doubtful, the hukm does not become actual without a qadi's ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, ownership transfers to the buyer before the qadi's ruling; under Muhammad, it does not.
This affects the validity of subsequent transactions and rights of parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا باع لا ينفذ بيعه وإن كان فيه مصلحة أجازه الحاكم لأنه مكلف عاقل، وينفذ فيما يضره كالإعتاق والطلاق ولو باع قبل حجر القاضي عليه جاز عند أبي حنيفة لأن السفيه ليس بمحبوس وإنما يستدل عليه بالعيون في تصرفاته، وذلك يحتمل لأنه يجوز أن يكون للسفيه ويجوز أن يكون حيلة عند الاستجلاب قلوب المجاهدين فإذا تردد لا يثبت حكمه إلا بقضاء القاضي بخلاف الجنون والصغر والعتة. وعند محمد لا يجوز لأن علة الحجر السفه وقد تحقق في الحال فيترتب عليه موجبه بغير قضاء كالصبا والجنون والعتة بخلاف الحجر بالدين لأنه لحق الغير لأنه من طلبهم.
And if he sells, his sale is not effective, although if it has benefit, the judge may validate it, because he is a legally responsible sane person. But it is effective in what harms him, like manumission and divorce. If he sold before the judge's interdiction upon him, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa because the safih is not interdicted [until the judge's ruling]; rather, one deduces it from signs in his transactions. That is uncertain because it could be from foolishness or could be a trick to attract the hearts of mujahidin. So if it is doubtful, its ruling is not established except by the judge's decision, unlike insanity, minority, and imbecility. According to Muhammad, it is not permissible because the cause of interdiction is safah, and it is realized at the moment, so its consequence follows without a judge's decision, like minority, insanity, and imbecility, unlike interdiction due to debt, because it is for the right of others, since it is from their claim.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a sale by a safih before a qadi has formally imposed interdiction. The text first states the general rule: a safih's sale is not effective unless the qadi approves it for benefit, but it is effective in harmful acts like manumission and divorce (which are binding regardless). The key ikhtilaf is about sales before the qadi's ruling. Abu Hanifa holds that such a sale is valid because the safih is not actually interdicted until a qadi declares it; the signs of safah are ambiguous, so the default is permissibility. Therefore, a qadi's ruling is necessary to establish interdiction. Muhammad holds that the sale is invalid because the cause of interdiction (safah) is present regardless of a qadi's ruling, similar to insanity or minority. The difference highlights the weight given to judicial declaration versus factual capacity in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
سفيه (safih): A person who is not prudent, i.e., spends wealth foolishly.
حجر القاضي (hajr al-qadi): The act of a judge declaring a person interdicted, thus restricting his financial autonomy.
عتة (utah): Imbecility, a state of mental deficiency short of insanity that affects capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because safah is a hidden condition
B) Because the safih is not actually interdicted until a judge rules, and his capacity is presumed intact
C) Because the safih is an adult
D) Because the buyer is unaware

Key Takeaway
The validity of a safih's sale before qadi interdiction hinges on whether safah is a self-evident cause (Muhammad) or requires judicial declaration (Abu Hanifa).

Tadbir by an Interdicted Person and the Obligation of Sa'y
Ruling on validity of tadbir by interdicted person and slave's obligation to work for value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of tadbir by an interdicted person and explain the dispute over the slave's obligation of sa'y.

Core Idea
Tadbir by an interdicted person is valid, but the slave's sa'y is disputed among the Hanafi imams.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two interconnected issues: the validity of a declaration of tadbir by a person interdicted due to safah, and the obligation of the mudabbar slave to work (sa'y) for his value. While Abu Hanifa upholds the validity of the tadbir, the three imams differ on whether the slave must perform sa'y immediately or after the master's death. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school balances the interdicted person's limited authority with the rights of creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
The passage covers: (a) validity of tadbir by mahjur 'alayh, (b) obligation of sa'y on the mudabbar slave.
Both issues are linked; the dispute is specifically on the sa'y.
2
State the common Hanafi position on tadbir validity
According to Abu Hanifa, tadbir is valid even from an interdicted person. The text states 'ولو دبر جاز تدبيره عنده' (if he declares tadbir, his tadbir is valid according to him).
This is the agreed position among the three imams; the dispute is on the sa'y, not the tadbir itself.
3
Map the intra-madhhab disagreement on sa'y
Abu Hanifa: no sa'y while master is alive, but after death if no rushd, sa'y is required. Abu Yusuf: first opinion – if tadbir was permitted in dhihar, no sa'y; later opinion – sa'y is obligatory (agreeing with Muhammad). Muhammad: sa'y is obligatory in all cases once tadbir is executed.
The later opinion of Abu Yusuf aligns with Muhammad's view.
4
Identify the tarjih (preferred view)
The text declares Muhammad's view as 'الصحيح' (the correct one) and explains the reasoning: sa'y is for the right of others (creditors), not for the master himself.
The mufta bihi position is that sa'y is obligatory after the master's death if no rushd.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider an interdicted person who declares tadbir and dies without regaining rushd. The slave must work to pay his value to the estate, which goes to creditors. If the master had regained rushd before death, sa'y might not be required? (The passage doesn't specify, but by analogy, rushd lifts interdiction, so tadbir becomes like a normal master's tadbir.)
The key is that sa'y is for the benefit of others, not the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا نفذ عندهما فعلى العبد أن يسعى في قيمته عند محمد وهو الصحيح، ولو جوز في الظهار نفذ ويسعى العبد في قيمته وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً، وفي قول أبي يوسف الأخير وهو رواية عن محمد عليه سعاية لأنه لو سعى لمعتقه والمعتق لا يلزمه السعاية لحق معتقه بحال ،ما وإنما تلزمه السعاية لأجل الغير. يسعى ولو دبر جاز تدبيره عنده إلا أن المدبر لا تجب عليه السعاية ما دام المولى حياً، فإذا مات المولى ولم يؤنس منه الرشد سعى في قيمته ،مدبراً
And if it is executed according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), then the slave must work to pay his value according to Muhammad, and that is the correct view. And if he permitted in dhihar, it is executed and the slave works for his value, and that is the first opinion of Abu Yusuf. And in the later opinion of Abu Yusuf, which is a narration from Muhammad, he must perform sa'y, because if he worked for his manumitter, the manumitter would not be obliged to perform sa'y due to the right of his manumitter at all, but sa'y is only required for the sake of another. He performs sa'y. And if he declares tadbir, his tadbir is valid according to him (Abu Hanifa), except that the mudabbar is not obliged to perform sa'y as long as the master is alive. When the master dies and his rationality has not been perceived, he works for his value as a mudabbar.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of tadbir (declaration of freedom upon death) by a person who is interdicted due to foolishness (safah). It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the tadbir is executed (i.e., becomes effective), the slave must work (sa'y) to pay his value, and this is the correct view. Abu Yusuf initially held that if the interdicted person permitted the slave to perform dhihar (a type of divorce), the tadbir is executed and the slave works for his value. However, in his later opinion, which is also narrated from Muhammad, the slave must perform sa'y because if he were to work for his manumitter, the manumitter would not be obligated to perform sa'y for his own right; rather, sa'y is required for the right of another (creditors). The passage also clarifies that if the interdicted person declares tadbir, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa, but the mudabbar slave is not required to work for value as long as the master is alive. After the master dies and if he has not shown rationality (rushd), the slave works for his value as a mudabbar.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by a master that his slave will be free upon the master's death.
سعاية (sa'y): The slave's work to earn money to pay his value to the master's estate.
محجور عليه (mahjur 'alayh): A person legally interdicted from managing his property due to foolishness (safah) or other reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The tadbir is invalid and the slave remains a slave.
B) The tadbir is valid but the slave does not have to work for value.
C) The tadbir is valid and the slave must work (sa'y) for his value.
D) The tadbir is suspended until the interdiction is lifted.

Key Takeaway
Tadbir by an interdicted person is valid, but the slave must perform sa'y after the master's death if no rushd, as per the preferred Hanafi view.

Imprisonment for Debt and Forced Sale: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on debtor imprisonment and forced sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on imprisoning a debtor and forced sale of his property, and evaluate the dalil supporting each view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa imprisons to compel payment; the Sahibayn also force the sale and interdict the debtor.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the rights of creditors against an insolvent debtor. While all agree that imprisonment is permissible, Abu Hanifa stops there, viewing forced sale as coercion. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad go further, allowing the judge to interdict the debtor and forcibly sell his property, drawing on the precedent of the Prophet's treatment of Mu'adh's debt. Understanding this difference illuminates the Hanafi spectrum between protecting the debtor's autonomy and ensuring repayment.

Guided Path
1
State the common ground
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that a procrastinating (mumatal) debtor can be imprisoned by the judge.
2
Identify the point of disagreement
Abu Hanifa opposes forced sale and interdiction; the Sahibayn permit them.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's dalil
The hadith of Mu'adh and the rationale of preventing harm (like talji'ah) support their view.
4
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa sees imprisonment as a means to compel payment, but forced sale would be coercion, which is not allowed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The view of the Sahibayn is the operative position for fatwa as it better protects creditors.
The author presents their view with dalil, indicating acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحبس ليبيع ماله في دينه لأن قضاء الدين واجب عليه والمماطلة ظلم فيحبسه الحاكم دفعاً لظلمه وإيطالاً للحق إلى مستحقه ولا يكون ذلك إكراهاً على البيع لأن المقصود من الحبس الحمل على قضاء الدين بأي طريق كان عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : إذا طلب غرماء المفلس الحجر عليه حجر عليه القاضي وباع ماله إن امتنع من بيعه وقسم ماله بين الغرماء ومنعه من تصرف يضر بالغرماء كالإقرار وبيعه بأقل من قيمته لما روي أن معاذاً ركبه دين فباع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ماله وقسم ثمنه بين غرمائه بالحصص، ولأن في الحجر عليه نظراً للغرماء لئلا يلحقهم الضرر بالإقرار والتلجئة أن وهو يبيعه من إنسان عظيم القدر لا يمكن الانتزاع منه أو بالإقرار له ثم
And he is imprisoned so that his property is sold for his debt, because repaying debt is obligatory upon him and procrastination is oppression, so the ruler imprisons him to remove his oppression and to give the right to its owner, and that is not considered coercion to sell because the purpose of imprisonment is to compel him to repay the debt by any means according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If the creditors of an insolvent person request interdiction upon him, the judge interdicts him, sells his property if he refuses to sell it himself, divides his property among the creditors, and prevents him from any disposition that harms the creditors, such as confession [of debt to a false creditor] or selling at less than its value. This is based on the report that Mu'adh was overtaken by debt, so the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, sold his property and divided its price among his creditors proportionally. And because in interdiction upon him there is consideration for the creditors lest harm befall them through confession or collusion (talji'ah), such that he sells it to a person of great status from whom it cannot be taken, or by confession to him then...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of imprisoning a debtor to force the sale of his property. According to Abu Hanifa, the judge can imprison the debtor to compel payment, but the sale is not forced; the debtor must sell voluntarily. The imprisonment is not considered coercion to sell; the goal is to pressure him to pay by any means. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if the creditors of an insolvent person request interdiction, the judge can interdict, sell the debtor's property forcibly if he refuses, divide the proceeds among creditors, and prevent him from transactions that harm them (like false confessions or selling below value). They support this with the hadith of Mu'adh, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) sold his property and distributed it among creditors. The passage also mentions the danger of talji'ah (collusive sale to a powerful person). This shows a clear difference: Abu Hanifa allows imprisonment but not forced sale or interdiction, while the Sahibayn allow both interdiction and forced sale.

Key Terms
مماطلة (mumatalah): Procrastination in repaying debt despite ability.
تلتجئة (talji'ah): A collusive sale to a powerful person to avoid creditors.
الحجر (al-hajr): Interdiction: a legal restriction on a person's capacity to manage property, imposed by a judge to protect creditors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only imprison him until he sells voluntarily.
B) Sell his property forcibly and distribute proceeds among creditors.
C) Assign a guardian to manage his property.
D) Nothing, as the debtor's property is inviolable.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school allows a judge to interdict and forcibly sell an insolvent debtor's property to satisfy creditors.
