Debt Payment When Currency Types Differ: The Judge's Authority Through Istihsān
Explains the ruling when debtor's property is in a different currency than the debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on selling a debtor's different currency and apply the istihsān reasoning to resolve the conflicting analogies.

Core Idea
When currency types differ, istihsān overrides qiyās to allow the judge's sale, balancing the unity of money with formal difference.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a practical debt repayment scenario: what if the debtor has dirhams but owes dinars? The judge must sell the currency to repay. While the Sahibayn see this as clearly permissible by qiyās, Imam Abu Hanifa arrives at the same outcome via istihsān (juristic preference), acknowledging that strict analogy would forbid it. This lesson unpacks the competing analogies and the principle that balances them, showing how legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school enriches its legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and consensus
Read the opening: the judge sells the debtor's dirhams to pay dinars (or vice versa). Note that this ruling is agreed upon by consensus (bil-ijma') within the Hanafi school.
Consensus here means agreement on the permissibility of the sale, not on the reasoning.
2
Distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Imam Abu Hanifa says the sale is allowed by istihsān, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) say it is clearly allowed by qiyās. Understand that both reach the same conclusion but through different methods.
The Sahibayn find no conflict in analogies; Imam Abu Hanifa sees a conflict resolved by istihsān.
3
Understand the qiyās that would prohibit
The text states that qiyās would forbid the sale because the debtor's property (dirhams) is not specifically designated (mut'ayyin) for repayment of a dīnār debt. It becomes like commodities (al-'urud), which the judge cannot sell absent specific authority.
This shows the default analogy that Imam Abu Hanifa must overcome.
4
Examine the istihsān reasoning
Istihsān considers two aspects: (a) unity – dirhams and dinars share the genus of thamaniyyah and māliyyah, so they are combined in zakāh; (b) difference – they differ in form and legally no ribā al-fadl applies between them. The unity gives the judge authority; the difference allows the creditor to accept the other currency.
This dual consideration (shabahayn) is the hallmark of istihsān here.
5
Connect to the practical outcome
The judge may sell, and the creditor receives payment. The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is minimal in this case—both sides agree on permissibility—but the methodological difference may affect other cases where analogies conflict.
The fatwa follows permissibility; the reasoning is important for legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ماله دراهم وله دنانير أو بالعكس بيع من دينه وهذا بالإجماع. أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عند الإمام فاستحسان به والقياس أن لا يجوز للقاضي بيعه لما ذكرنا أن هذا الطريق غير متعين لقضاء الدين فصار كالعروض وجه الاستحسان أنهما يتحدان جنساً الثمنية والمالية ولذا يضم أحدهما إلى الآخر في الزكاة يختلفان في الصور حقيقة وحكماً. أما حقيقة فظاهر، وأما حكماً فلأنه لا يجري بينهما ربا الفضل لاختلافهما فبالنظر إلى الاتحاد يثبت للقاضي ولاية التصرف، وبالنظر إلى الاختلاف يسكت عن الدائن فله الأخذ عملاً بالشبهين.
And if his property is dirhams and his debt is dinars, or vice versa, it is sold for his debt, and this is by consensus. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is apparent; and as for according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), it is by istihsan. The qiyas is that the judge is not permitted to sell it, because we mentioned that this path is not specifically designated for repayment of debt, so it becomes like commodities. The reason for istihsan is that they share the genus of being medium of exchange and monetary value, and therefore one is added to the other in zakah, though they differ in form, both in reality and legally. As for reality, it is apparent; as for legally, because riba al-fadl does not apply between them due to their difference. So with regard to their unity, the judge has the authority to dispose; and with regard to their difference, the creditor is allowed to take, acting on both aspects.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the power of the judge to sell the debtor's currency when it does not match the type of debt. The ruling: if the debtor possesses dirhams but owes dinars (or vice versa), the judge sells the currency to pay the debt – this is agreed upon (bil-ijma'). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn), it is clearly permissible. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is permissible by istihsan (juristic preference), whereas qiyas (analogy) would prohibit it because this method of payment is not specifically intended for debt repayment, similar to commodities. The istihsan argument: dirhams and dinars share the same genus as mediums of exchange and monetary value, thus they are combined in zakah; yet they differ in form and legally because no riba al-fadl applies between them. Considering their unity, the judge has authority; considering their difference, the creditor may take the other currency. The practical outcome: the judge can effect the sale and the creditor receives payment.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that abandons qiyas for a stronger consideration, here the unity of currency type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa allow the judge to sell dirhams to pay a dīnār debt by istihsān?
A) Because dirhams and dīnārs are identical in every respect.
B) Because they share the genus of being medium of exchange, yet differ in form, so both aspects are considered.
C) Because the creditor has the right to any property.
D) Because qiyās clearly allows it.

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa uses istihsān to permit the judge's sale of a debtor's different currency, balancing the unity of money with the difference in form.

Judge's Sale of Debtor's Goods and Real Estate
Expansion of Kanz al-Daqaiq on debtor's asset sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge selling a debtor's property and explain the order of sale according to the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The judge may sell a debtor's property to repay debts, starting with cash, then goods, then real estate.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author expands on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the judge's authority to sell a debtor's movable and immovable property. We will examine the initial position of Imam Abu Hanifa, the consensus on posthumous sale, and the detailed order prescribed by the Sahibayn, along with the practical exceptions for the debtor's basic necessities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The matn states that the judge does not sell the debtor's goods and real estate. This is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and applies both during life and after death.
After death, the sale is allowed by consensus; the disagreement is during life.
2
Note the consensus and fatwa
After death, sale is by consensus. During life, the fatwa (mufta bihi) is on the permissibility of the judge selling goods and real estate, as stated in al-Jawhara and al-Bazzaziyya.
The fatwa overrides Imam Abu Hanifa's apparent prohibition during life.
3
Detail the Sahibayn's order
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the judge sells in order: cash first (since it is for circulation), then goods (for profit), and finally real estate (only when necessary). A second narration prioritizes goods at risk of loss.
Real estate is sold only as a last resort due to its permanence and function as security.
4
Extract the exceptions
The debtor is left with minimal clothing (one or two sets) and a basic dwelling if needed. Only surplus property is sold.
The principle: debt repayment is obligatory (fard), so it takes precedence over unnecessary possessions.
5
Understand the rationale
The Sahibayn's order minimizes hardship for the debtor while ensuring the creditor's right. The judge must balance enforcement with mercy.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on istihsan (juristic preference) in financial transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يبع عرضه وعقاره) وهذا عند الإمام وهو بإطلاقه صادق بحال الحياة والموت قال في الجوهرة وبيع القاضي عرضه وعقاره بعد الموت بالإجماع. بيع القاضي ذلك وعليه الفتوى؛ كذا في البزازية فعندهما يبدأ القاضي ببيع النقود لأنها مفيدة للتقليب ولا ينتفع بعينها، فإن فضل شيء من الدين بيع العروض فيها لأنها مفيدة للتقليب والاسترباح فإن لم يف ثمنها بالدين بيع العقار لأن العقار مفيد للنفي عادة فلا يبيعه إلا عند الضرورة هذه الطريقة إحدى الروايتين عندهما، وفي الرواية الأخرى عندهما يبدأ القاضي ببيع ما يخشى عليه التوي من عروضه ثم ما لا يخشى عليه التلف منه، ثم بيع العقار ويترك عليه دست ثياب من ثياب بدنه ويبيع الباقي لأنه به كفاية . وقيل : يترك دستان لأنه إذا غسل ثيابه لا بد من ثياب يلبسها قالوا : إذا كان للمديون ثياب يلبسها ويكتفي بدونها يبيع ثيابه ويقضي الدين ببعض ثمنها ويشتري بما بقي ثوباً يلبيسه لأن قضاء الدين فرض عليه فكان أولى من التحمل وعلى هذا إذا كان له مسكن ويمكنه أن يجتزي بدون ذلك يبيع ذلك المسكن ويوفى ببعض ثمنه الدين ويشتري بالباقي مسكناً يسكن فيه، وعن هذا قال مشايخنا يبيع ما لا يحتاج إليه في الحال حتى يبيع اللبد في الصيف والنطع في الشتاء.
And (the judge) does not sell his goods and real estate) – this according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) and it applies in life and death. It is said in al-Jawhara: The judge sells his goods and real estate after death by consensus. The sale by the judge is the basis of fatwa, as in al-Bazzaziyya. According to them (Sahibayn), the judge begins with selling money because it is useful for circulation and one does not benefit from its substance. If something remains of the debt, goods are sold because they are useful for circulation and profit. If their price does not cover the debt, real estate is sold because real estate is usually for security; it is only sold when necessary. This is one of two narrations from them. In the other narration, the judge begins by selling goods that are feared to be lost, then those not feared to be lost, then real estate. He leaves for him one set of clothes for his body and sells the rest, because that suffices him. It is said: he leaves two sets because when he washes one, he needs clothes to wear. They said: if the debtor has clothes that he wears and can do without, he sells his clothes, pays part of the debt from their price, and buys a garment for his body, because repaying the debt is an obligation upon him, so it takes precedence over bearing the cost. Similarly, if he has a dwelling and can do without it, he sells that dwelling, pays some of the debt from its price, and buys a smaller dwelling to live in. From this our scholars said: he sells what he does not need at the moment, even selling the felt in summer and the mat in winter.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage details the procedure for the judge to sell a debtor's property to satisfy debts, with differences among the Hanafi imams. The initial statement 'ولم يبع عرضه وعقاره' is the matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq, meaning the judge does not sell the debtor's movable goods and real estate (during his lifetime) according to Imam Abu Hanifa – but this is restricted: selling after death is by consensus. The fatwa is on the permissibility of the judge selling these assets. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) specify an order: first sell cash (money), then goods, then real estate. There is also a second narration: sell goods at risk of loss first. Minimal clothing and housing are excluded; only surplus is sold. The principle: repaying debt is obligatory (fard), so it takes precedence over unnecessary possessions.

Key Terms
عرض (ard): Movable goods (merchandise) other than cash and real estate.
عقار (iqaar): Real estate (land and buildings).
دست ثياب (dast thiyab): One set of clothing needed for the debtor's body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Real estate first, then goods, then money.
B) Money first, then goods, then real estate.
C) Goods first, then money, then real estate.
D) Only real estate.

Key Takeaway
The judge may sell a debtor's goods and real estate to satisfy debts, following a prescribed order that minimizes hardship, while leaving basic necessities.

Imprisonment of the Insolvent Debtor and Creditors' Rights
Rulings on when an insolvent debtor is imprisoned and released

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on imprisonment of the insolvent debtor, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on mulāzama (creditors' pursuit), and identify the proof for each position.

Core Idea
Insolvent debtor is imprisoned but released if no assets appear; thereafter, Abu Hanifa allows pursuit, Sahibayn order respite.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical practical issue: what should be done with a debtor who cannot pay? The Hanafi school does not automatically place him under interdiction (hajr) but rather imprisons him until assets appear. A key dispute arises after release: can creditors continue to pursue him, or must they give him respite? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold different views, each supported by distinct proofs from hadith and Quran. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for applying the correct ruling in modern insolvency cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal measure for insolvency
The text states the debtor is not placed under hajr (interdiction) but is imprisoned. Note that imprisonment is a means to compel payment, not a punishment.
Hajr would restrict his legal capacity; imprisonment only restricts his freedom.
2
Determine what happens after release
If no assets appear, the debtor is released from prison. The key dispute concerns what rights creditors have after release.
3
Contrast Abu Hanifa's position with the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa allows creditors to pursue the debtor (mulāzama) and take surplus earnings proportionally. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the judge restrains the debtor from creditors unless they prove hidden assets.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Examine the proofs for each view
Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith 'the owner of a debt has the hand and the tongue' – hand means pursuit, tongue means litigation. The Sahibayn cite Quran 2:280: 'if the debtor is in difficulty, grant him respite until ease' – since insolvency is established, waiting is obligatory.
The hadith is ahad (zanni thubut); the Quranic verse is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalala.
5
Note the practical consequence on debtor's voluntary preference
According to the text, the debtor may voluntarily prefer one creditor over others because his property is not encumbered; the debt is in his liability (dhimma). This applies under both views.
This shows that the right is in the person, not the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإفلاس) يعني لا يحجر عليه بسبب الإفلاس بل يحبس حتى يظهر له مال فإن لم يظهر له أخرجه من الحبس، وقد ذكرنا الحبس وما يحبس فيه من الديون وكيفية الحبس وقدره وبدين من يحبس والملازمة وصفتها في كتاب القضاء. وإذا أخرجه من الحبس لا يحول بينه وبين غرمائه بعد الإخراج بل يلازمونه عند أبي حنيفة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الصاحب الحق اليد واللسان أراد باليد الملازمة وباللسان التقاضي. ويأخذون فضل كسبه ويقسم بينهم بالحصص لاستواء حقوقهم في القوة. ولو قدم البعض على البعض في القضاء جاز لأنه تصرف في خالص ملكه ولم يتعلق لأحد حق في ماله وإنما حقه في ذمته فله أن يؤثر من يشاء من غرمائه؛ ذكره في النهاية. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : إذا فلسه الحاكم حال بينه وبين غرمائه إلا أن يقيموا البينة أن له مالاً لقوله تعالى وإن كان ذو عسرة فنظرة إلى ميسرة [البقرة: ٢٨٠] وقد ثبت عسرته فوجب انتظاره وفي الهداية قال محمد المدعي أن يحبسه في بيته أو يتخذ حبسه، وفي رواية أخرى لرب الدين أن يلزم مديونه المعسر حيث أحب وإن كان الملزوم لا معيشة له إلا من يده لم يكن له أن يمنعه من الذهاب والمجيء.
And insolvency: meaning he is not placed under interdiction due to insolvency, but he is imprisoned until money appears; if it does not appear, he is released from prison. We have mentioned imprisonment, and for which debts one is imprisoned, and the manner and extent of imprisonment, and for which debt one is imprisoned, and the process of sequestration and its description in the Book of Judgments. When he is released from prison, he is not prevented from interacting with his creditors after release, but they may pursue him according to Abu Hanifa due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'The owner of a debt has the hand and the tongue.' By 'hand' he meant pursuit, and by 'tongue' he meant litigation. They take the surplus of his earnings and divide it among them in proportion because their rights are equal in strength. If he favors some over others in repayment, it is permissible because he is disposing of his own property, and no one has a right to his property; rather, their right is in his liability. So he may prefer whomever he wishes among his creditors; mentioned in al-Nihaya. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: if the judge declares him insolvent, he prevents him from dealing with his creditors unless they prove that he has money, due to His statement: 'And if [the debtor] is in difficulty, then grant him respite until ease' [Quran 2:280]. And his difficulty has been established, so waiting is obligatory. In al-Hidayah, Muhammad said: the claimant may imprison him in his house or designate a prison for him. In another narration, the creditor may compel his debtor wherever he wants, but if the compelled person has no livelihood except from his own hands, he is not prevented from coming and going.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on insolvency (iflās) in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings on the debtor who cannot pay. The key point: the debtor is not placed under 'hajr' (interdiction) simply for being insolvent; rather, he is imprisoned until he reveals assets. If no assets appear, he is released. Once released, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, creditors may pursue him (mulāzama) based on the hadith 'the owner of the debt has the hand and the tongue' – meaning the creditor can keep track and demand payment. They can take the surplus of his earnings proportionally. Interestingly, the debtor may voluntarily prefer one creditor over others because his property is not encumbered; the debt is in his liability. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ: if the judge declares him insolvent, he restrains him from dealing with creditors unless they prove he has money, citing the Quranic verse of waiting. This is a protection for the debtor. There is also a narration from Muhammad about house arrest. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, creditors actively pursue; under Sahibayn, the judge protects the debtor from harassment unless he has hidden assets.

Key Terms
ملازمة (mulāzama): The creditor's right to physically pursue or keep track of the debtor to ensure payment.
إفلاس (iflās): Legal insolvency where debtor's liabilities exceed assets or he cannot pay.
عسر (usur): Financial difficulty or hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He is placed under complete interdiction and cannot transact.
B) He is free from all obligations.
C) Creditors may still pursue him and take surplus earnings.
D) He must be followed by a judge at all times.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an insolvent debtor is not interdicted but imprisoned; after release, there is a dispute on whether creditors may pursue him, with Abu Hanifa allowing mulāzama based on hadith and the Sahibayn granting respite based on Quran.

Seller's Right When Buyer Becomes Insolvent
Dispute on seller's right to reclaim goods from insolvent buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the seller's right when the buyer becomes insolvent, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
After delivery, the seller is just another creditor; can't reclaim specific goods.

When a buyer takes delivery of goods but fails to pay and becomes insolvent, a natural question arises: can the seller take back his goods? The Hanafi school holds that ownership has transferred, so the seller becomes an ordinary creditor. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, citing a hadith that grants the seller priority. This lesson walks through the evidence and the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the matn: the seller is equal with other creditors (uswat al-ghuramā'). Note the exception: before delivery or if possession was unauthorized, the seller can withhold or reclaim.
The ruling applies only after the buyer takes delivery with the seller's permission.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the seller has the right to rescind the contract (faskh al-aqd) and reclaim the goods both before and after delivery.
This is a direct conflict on the permissibility of rescission after delivery.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the hadith from Muslim 'Whoever finds his property in the possession of a man who has become insolvent... he has more right to it' and the qiyas (analogy) to the seller's inability to deliver the goods.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Hurayra and is sahih.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi school limits the hadith: it applies only before delivery or when possession was unauthorized. After delivery, the buyer is the owner, so the seller's claim is a debt, not ownership.
The word 'أحق' is interpreted as priority in distribution, not ownership.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Shafi'i view, the seller gets the full asset; under the Hanafi view, the seller shares proportionally with all creditors.
This difference affects the seller's recovery in insolvency proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أفلس مبتاع عين فبائعه أسوة الغرماء) يعني لو اشترى متاعاً فأفلس والمتاع في يده فالذي باعه المتاع أسوة الغرماء فيه مراده بعد قبض المشتري المتاع بإذن البائع، وإن كان قبل القبض فللبائع أن يحبس المتاع حتى يقبض الثمن، وكذا إذا قبضه بغير إذن البائع كان له أن يسترده ويحبسه بالثمن. وقال الإمام الشافعي : للبائع فسخ العقد وأخذ متاعه قبل القبض وبعده لما أخرجه الأمام مسلم عن أبي هريرة قال عليه الصلاة والسلام من أدرك ماله بعينه عند رجل أفلس أو عند إنسان قد أفلس فهو أحق به من غيره. ولأن المشتري قد عجز عن تسليم إحدى بدلي العقد وهو الثمن فيثبت للبائع حق الفسخ كما إذا عجز عن تسليم المبيع. والجامع بينهما أنه
And if the buyer of a specific commodity becomes insolvent, then his seller is equal with the creditors. That is, if he bought goods and becomes insolvent while the goods are in his possession, the seller of the goods is equal with the creditors regarding it – meaning after the buyer took delivery with the seller's permission. If before delivery, the seller may withhold the goods until he receives the price. Similarly, if he took delivery without the seller's permission, he may reclaim it and withhold it for the price. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The seller has the right to rescind the contract and take his goods before and after delivery, because of what Imam Muslim narrated from Abu Hurayra: he said, peace be upon him: 'Whoever finds his property in the possession of a man who has become insolvent, or a person who has become insolvent, he has more right to it than others.' And because the buyer has become unable to deliver one of the two equivalents of the contract, which is the price, so the seller has the right to rescind, just as if he were unable to deliver the sold item. And the common link between them is that...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the rights of a seller when the buyer becomes insolvent after taking possession of the goods. The Hanafi position (as per the matn 'وإن أفلس مبتاع عين فبائعه أسوة الغرماء'): the seller is treated like other creditors; he cannot reclaim the specific goods but must share proportionally with other creditors. However, there are exceptions: before delivery, the seller may withhold the goods; if possession was without permission, he may reclaim. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the seller can rescind the contract and reclaim his goods both before and after delivery, based on the sahih hadith from Abu Hurayra: 'Whoever finds his property in the possession of a man who has become insolvent, he has more right to it than others.' He also uses qiyas: if the buyer cannot pay, it is like the seller being unable to deliver the goods, which gives the other party the right to rescind. The Hanafi response (not fully quoted here) would likely limit the hadith to cases before delivery or without permission, or interpret 'he has more right' as priority in distribution, not ownership. The key difference: Hanafis consider the buyer's ownership complete upon delivery, so the seller merely has a debt claim; Shafi'is allow reclaiming the specific asset.

Key Terms
أسوة الغرماء (uswat al-ghuramā'): Equal with the creditors; the seller has no priority over other creditors.
فسخ العقد (faskh al-aqd): Rescission of the contract, allowing seller to take back goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when can a seller reclaim his goods from a buyer who becomes insolvent?
A) Only before the buyer takes delivery with permission.
B) Both before and after delivery.
C) After delivery if the seller wishes.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
After delivery with permission, the seller cannot reclaim goods from an insolvent buyer; he is equal with other creditors.

The Seller's Status When the Buyer Becomes Bankrupt: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains the Hanafi ruling on the seller's status among creditors and rebuts Shafi'i position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the seller's status when the buyer becomes bankrupt, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on Quran and hadith interpretation.

Core Idea
A seller becomes an ordinary creditor when a bankrupt buyer has taken possession with permission – no rescission.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute regarding the seller's right to rescind the sale when the buyer becomes bankrupt. The Hanafi school holds that the seller is merely an ordinary creditor if the buyer has taken possession with permission, while the Shafi'i school argues that the seller can reclaim the goods. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position, cites the Shafi'i proof from a hadith and qiyas, and then rebuts it using Quran 2:280 and a reinterpretation of the hadith. Understanding this dispute reveals how different schools weigh textual evidence and apply principles of contract and insolvency.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: if the buyer has taken possession of the goods with the seller's permission and then becomes bankrupt, the seller is like other creditors (uswah al-ghurama). Note the exceptions: no possession or possession without permission allows the seller to reclaim.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Understand that Imam al-Shafi'i allows the seller to rescind the contract and take back the goods in all cases, whether before or after possession.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: a hadith from Muslim ('Whoever finds his property with a bankrupt person is more entitled to it') and a qiyas (analogy) to the case where the seller cannot deliver the sold item.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Study how the Hanafis counter: (a) Quran 2:280 defers the debt until ease, so no immediate right to rescind; (b) the hadith is interpreted to refer to the buyer's property (ownership transferred), not the seller's; (c) the qiyas fails because the situations are not truly analogous.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: under Hanafi law, the seller must queue with other creditors; under Shafi'i law, the seller can reclaim the specific asset.

قال رحمه الله : (وإن أفلس مبتاع عين فبائعه أسوة الغرماء) يعني لو اشترى متاعاً فأفلس والمتاع في يده فالذي باعه المتاع أسوة الغرماء فيه مراده بعد قبض المشتري المتاع بإذن البائع، وإن كان قبل القبض فللبائع أن يحبس المتاع حتى يقبض الثمن، وكذا إذا قبضه بغير إذن البائع كان له أن يسترده ويحبسه بالثمن. وقال الإمام الشافعي : للبائع فسخ العقد وأخذ متاعه قبل القبض وبعده لما أخرجه الأمام مسلم عن أبي هريرة قال عليه الصلاة والسلام من أدرك ماله بعينه عند رجل أفلس أو عند إنسان قد أفلس فهو أحق به من غيره (۱) . ولأن المشتري قد عجز عن تسليم إحدى بدلي العقد وهو الثمن فيثبت للبائع حق الفسخ كما إذا عجز عن تسليم المبيع. والجامع بينهما أنه عقد معاوضة فيقتضي المساواة وإنما قوله تعالى وإن كان ذو عسرة فنظرة إلى ميسرة [البقرة: ٢٨٠] فاستحق النظر إلى الميسرة بالآية فليس له المطالبة قبلها ولا فسخ بدون المطالبة بالثمن، وهذا لأن الدين صار مؤجلاً إلى الميسرة بتأجيل الشارع وبالعجر عن الدين المؤجل من المتعاقدين لا يجب له خيار الفسخ قبل مضي الأجل فكيف يثبت ذلك في تأجيل الشارع وهو أقوى من تأجيلهما والجواب عن الحديث أنه قال من وجد ماله» وهذا مال المشتري لا مال البائع وإنما يصلح أن يكون حجة أن لو قال فأصاب رجل عين مال قد كان باعه من الذي وجده في يده ولم يقبض ثمنه فهو أحق به من كل الغرماء، وهو نظير ما روي عن سمرة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال من سرق ماله أو ضاع له متاع فوجده في يد رجل بعينه فهو أحق به ويرجع المشتري على بائعه بالثمن رواه الطحاوي. وقوله «عقد معاوضة» فيقتضي المساواة قلنا : يقتضي التسوية بينهما في الملك وهو لكل واحد منهما ولئن سلمنا أنه يفيد التسوية في القبض فقد بطل ذلك بالتأجيل إلى الميسرة
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And if the buyer of a specific asset becomes bankrupt, then his seller is like the other creditors.) Meaning: if someone purchases goods and becomes bankrupt while the goods are in his possession, then the one who sold him the goods is like the other creditors therein. This is intended after the buyer has taken possession of the goods with the seller's permission. If it was before possession, the seller may detain the goods until he receives the price. Likewise, if he took possession without the seller's permission, the seller may reclaim them and detain them for the price. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The seller has the right to rescind the contract and take back his goods before and after possession, due to what Imam Muslim narrated from Abu Hurayra: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Whoever finds his property with a man who has become bankrupt, or with a person who has become bankrupt, then he is more entitled to it than others.' Also, because the buyer is unable to deliver one of the two exchange items (the price), so the seller's right to rescind is established, just as if the seller were unable to deliver the sold item. The common factor between them is that it is a commutative contract, thus it necessitates equality. However, Allah's words: 'And if someone is in hardship, then deferment until ease' (2:280) entitle the debtor to respite until ease by the verse, so the creditor cannot demand payment before that, nor rescind without demanding the price. This is because the debt becomes deferred until ease by the Legislator's deferment, and inability to pay a deferred debt by one of the parties does not give the other an option to rescind before the term expires, so how can that be established when the Legislator's deferment is stronger than their own? The answer to the hadith is that he said 'whoever finds his property' – and this is the buyer's property, not the seller's. It would be evidence only if he had said: 'If a man finds specific property that he had sold to someone and finds it in his possession without having received its price, then he is more entitled to it than all creditors.' This is similar to what was narrated from Samura: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Whoever has his property stolen or lost, then finds it in the possession of a person, he is more entitled to it, and the buyer may sue the seller for the price' – reported by al-Tahawi. Regarding his statement 'a commutative contract necessitates equality' – we say: it necessitates equality between them in ownership, which each has. And if we grant that it necessitates equality in possession, then that is invalidated by the deferment to ease.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the rights of a seller when the buyer of a specific asset becomes bankrupt (iflas). The Hanafi position is stated first: if the buyer has taken possession of the goods with the seller's permission and then becomes bankrupt, the seller is treated as an ordinary creditor (uswah al-ghurama) – he cannot reclaim the goods but must share proportionately with other creditors. However, if the buyer has not taken possession, the seller may detain the goods until payment. Likewise, if the buyer took possession without the seller's permission, the seller may rescind and reclaim. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the seller always has the right to rescind the contract and take back the goods, whether before or after possession, citing a hadith from Muslim: 'Whoever finds his property with a bankrupt person is more entitled to it.' He also uses a qiyas (analogy): just as inability to deliver the sold item gives the buyer a right to rescind, inability to pay the price should give the seller a right to rescind. The Hanafi response invokes Quran 2:280 which defers the debt until ease, making the debt effectively deferred. Since the debt is deferred by the Legislator, it is even stronger than a contractual deferment, which does not give rescission rights before term. The Hanafis also interpret the hadith differently: the hadith refers to 'his property' which is the buyer's property, not the seller's. They argue that the hadith would be applicable only if the seller's property were still in the buyer's possession without the price having been paid – but that is not the case here because the property has been sold and is now the buyer's. They cite a hadith from Samura about stolen or lost property, which supports the idea that one can reclaim one's own property if found in someone else's hands. The Hanafi position also notes that the commutative contract (عقد معاوضة) establishes equality in ownership (each party owns the exchanged item), but this does not imply equality in possession – the buyer's possession is valid. The deferment to ease overrides any supposed equality in possession.

Key Terms
أسوة الغرماء (uswah al-ghuramā'): The seller is treated as an ordinary creditor, sharing proportionately in the bankrupt's assets.
إفلاس (iflās): Bankruptcy: a legal state where a debtor's liabilities exceed assets, leading to interdiction (hajr) in some cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the seller of a specific asset have priority over other creditors if the buyer becomes bankrupt?
A) Always, because the seller can rescind the contract.
B) Only if the buyer had not taken possession of the asset with the seller's permission.
C) Only if the buyer had taken possession with permission.
D) Never; the seller is always an ordinary creditor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a seller who has delivered goods to a buyer with permission becomes an ordinary creditor if the buyer becomes bankrupt; only if possession was not taken or was without permission can the seller reclaim the goods.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
The Permitted Slave: Definition, Conditions, and Ruling
Defines the chapter's subject, its evidence, conditions, and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition, evidence, cause, pillar, conditions, and ruling of the permission (idhn) for a slave to trade according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The permission (idhn) for a slave to trade is a release from interdiction, requiring a cause, a pillar, and specific conditions.

This lesson introduces the chapter on the ma'dhun – a slave permitted to trade. We will explore why this chapter follows interdiction, unpack the linguistic and legal meaning of idhn, and examine the evidence from the Quran. We will then detail the cause, pillar, conditions, and ruling that make this permission valid and effective in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven aspects of the chapter
The passage lists seven aspects: linguistic meaning, evidence, cause, pillar, conditions, interpretation, and ruling. Understand each as a building block.
This structure is typical in Hanafi fiqh works.
2
Define idhn linguistically and legally
Linguistically, idhn means release (itlaq), the opposite of hajr (interdiction). Legally, it is the removal of the barrier preventing someone from acting. Note the difference with Zaylai's view that it means informing.
Imam al-Zaylai's alternative linguistic definition is noted but not adopted.
3
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The verse 'And seek from the bounty of Allah' (62:10) is cited. Permission for a slave to trade is an application of this general command.
Evidence is Quranic (dalil naqli).
4
Analyze the cause, pillar, and conditions
The cause is need (hajah). The pillar is the owner's statement 'I have permitted you'. Conditions include: the granter must have authority, the slave must be rational, discerning, knowledgeable about trade, and must be informed of the permission.
Conditions are from al-Muhit and other sources.
5
Understand the ruling and its scope
The ruling (hukm) is that the ma'dhun owns trade-related transactions and their accessories, but not other matters. This distinguishes his capacity from full ownership.
The commentator critiques a competing view that equates the ruling with legal interpretation.

كتاب المأذون تأخير كتاب المأذون عقيب كتاب الحجر ظاهر لأن الإذن يقتضي سبق الحجر، ولما ترتب وجوداً ترتب أيضاً ذكراً للتناسب. والكلام هنا من وجوه الأول في معناه لغة. الثاني في دليل المشروعية الثالث في سببه الرابع في ركنه الخامس في شرطه. السادس في تفسيره. السابع في حكمه. أما معناه لغة قال شيخ الإسلام خواهر زاده في مبسوطة : الإذن الإطلاق لغة لأنه ضد الحجر وهو المنع فكان إطلاقاً عن شيء إلى شيء كان ا هـ . وفي النهاية : أما اللغة فالإذن في الشيء رفع المانع لمن هو محجور عنه وإعلام بإطلاقه فيما حجر عليه من أذن له في الشيء اذناً. وأبعد الإمام الزيلعي حيث قال : والإذن في اللغة الإعلام الأذان وهو الإعلام لأن الأذن أذن كذا إذا أباحه وأذان في من من أذن بكذا إذا أعلم وبينهما فرق . وأما دليل المشروعية فهو قوله تعالى وابتغوا من فضل الله ﴾ [الجمعة: ١٠] وإذن الصبي والعبد في التجارة ابتغاء من فضل الله . وأما سبب المشروعية فهو الحاجة لأن الإنسان قد لا يتفرغ لذلك بنفسه لكثرة اشتغاله فيحتاج أن يستعين بالعبد والصغير. وأما ركنه فقول المولى لعبده أذنت لك في هذا. وأما شرائطه ففي المحيط شرائط جوازه فولاية الأذن على المأذون حجراً وإطلاقاً منعاً وإسقاطاً كون المأذون عاقلاً مميزاً عالماً عارفاً بما يؤذن له وأن يعلم العبد بالإذن. وفي السغناقي : دخل في قولنا من له ولاية الإذن في التجارة المكاتب والمأذون والمضارب والشريك مفاوضة والأب والجد والقاضي والولي ا هـ. وأما حكمه قال في غاية البيان : فملك المأذون كل ما كان من قبيل التجارة وتوابعها وعدم ملكه ما لم يكن كذلك وعزاه إلى التحفة وأبعد صاحب النهاية والإمام الزيلعي حيث قالا : وأما حكمه فهو تفسيره الشرعي لأن حكم الشيء ما يثبت به ولا يذهب على ذي مسكة أن ما يثبت بالشيء أثراً مرتباً عليه لا يصلح أن يكون تفسيراً لذلك الشيء محمولاً عليه بالمواطأة. وأما ويصير
Book of the Permitted Slave. The placement of the Book of the Permitted Slave after the Book of Interdiction is apparent because permission requires prior interdiction. Since they are ordered in existence, they are also ordered in mention for harmony. The discussion here has several aspects: first, its linguistic meaning; second, the evidence of its legislation; third, its cause; fourth, its pillar; fifth, its conditions; sixth, its interpretation; seventh, its ruling. As for its linguistic meaning: Shaykh al-Islam Khaharzadah said in his Mabsut: 'Idhn linguistically means release, because it is the opposite of hajr which is prevention. So it is a release from something to something else.' In al-Nihayah: 'As for language, idhn in a thing is the removal of the barrier from one who is interdicted from it, and informing of his release in what he was interdicted from. One says: adhina lahu fi al-shay'i idhnan.' Imam al-Zaylai went far when he said: 'Idhn linguistically means informing (i'lam); adhan means informing, because al-adhn means permission, and adhan fi min means he informed, and there is a difference between them.' As for the evidence of its legislation, it is Allah's saying: 'And seek from the bounty of Allah' [al-Jumu'ah:10]; and permission for the child and the slave in trade is seeking from the bounty of Allah. As for the cause of its legislation, it is need, because a person may not be free to do it himself due to his many preoccupations, so he needs to seek help from the slave and the minor. As for its pillar, it is the owner's saying to his slave: 'I have permitted you in this.' As for its conditions, in al-Muhit: the conditions of its validity are: the one who has authority to give permission over the ma'dhun in terms of interdiction and release, prohibition and removal; that the ma'dhun be rational, discerning, knowledgeable, and aware of what he is permitted for; and that the slave be informed of the permission. In al-Sighnaqi: 'Included in our statement "the one who has authority to give permission in trade" are: the mukatib, the ma'dhun, the mudarib, the sharik al-mufawada, the father, the grandfather, the judge, and the guardian.' As for its ruling, it is said in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'The ma'dhun owns everything that is of the type of trade and its accessories, and does not own what is not like that.' He attributed it to al-Tuhfah. The author of al-Nihayah and Imam al-Zaylai went far when they said: 'As for its ruling, it is its legal interpretation, because the ruling of a thing is what is established by it, and it does not escape anyone possessing reason that what is established by a thing as an effect arranged upon it cannot be an interpretation of that thing carried upon it by agreement.' And as for...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the ma'dhun, a slave who is permitted to engage in trade. The commentator first explains the placement of this chapter after the book of interdiction, noting that permission logically presupposes interdiction. Then he lists seven aspects to be discussed: linguistic meaning, evidence, cause, pillar, conditions, interpretation, and ruling. Linguistically, idhn means release or permission, the opposite of hajr (interdiction). The evidence for its legislation is the Quranic verse 'And seek from the bounty of Allah' (62:10), as permission for a slave or minor to trade is a form of seeking Allah's bounty. The cause for legislation is human need: the owner cannot always attend to trade personally and may delegate to a slave or minor. The pillar (rukn) is the owner's explicit declaration of permission. The conditions for validity include that the permissive authority has the right to interdict or release, and that the ma'dhun is rational, discerning, and knowledgeable about trade. The condition also requires the slave to be informed of the permission. The ruling is that the ma'dhun owns all trade-related transactions and their accessories but does not own what is not of that type. The commentator notes a disagreement between scholars on whether the ruling itself is the legal interpretation; he sides with the view that the ruling is what is established by the permission, not the interpretation itself.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his owner to engage in trade.
الإذن (al-idhn): Linguistically meaning release, and legally meaning the removal of the barrier of interdiction, allowing someone to act in a particular domain.
الحجر (al-hajr): Interdiction; preventing someone from disposing of their property due to incapacity (minority, insanity, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the pillar (rukn) of giving permission to a slave to trade?
A) The slave's acceptance
B) The owner's statement 'I have permitted you in this'
C) A written contract
D) Witnesses

Key Takeaway
The permission for a slave to trade is a structured legal institution requiring a specific cause, pillar, conditions, and producing a limited ownership over trade-related transactions.
